The civic return is harder to quantify — but the relationship between asset ownership and political participation is one of the strongest predictors in the political science literature: Soss and Jacobs (2009) document that asset-owning citizens participate at consistently higher rates than equivalent-income non-asset-owning citizens, even after controlling for income, education, and political interest.

**3f. Housing as a justiciable right with enforceable production mandate.**

Housing is the material foundation of every other domain in this proposal. Citizens without stable housing cannot participate in citizens' assemblies, hold jobs that enable union organizing, send their children to school consistently enough to benefit from the education reforms in Domain 10, or participate in democratic processes that require stable addresses for voter registration. The 650,000+ Americans experiencing homelessness on a given night in 2024 (HUD Point-in-Time Count, 2024, the highest recorded) are not a peripheral issue in a democracy renewal proposal — they are evidence of the most acute form of democratic exclusion that exists: people for whom the formal rights of citizenship have been rendered inoperable by material conditions.

The United States has never recognized housing as a justiciable right. The failure is structural and deliberate: the Supreme Court's decision in Lindsey v. Normet (1972) explicitly rejected a constitutional right to housing, and no subsequent federal court has recognized one. The result is that housing policy exists entirely in the domain of legislative discretion — subject to the same donor-class capture that the fiscal and electoral reforms in this document address. When housing policy is determined by legislatures in which real estate interests are the largest single source of campaign contributions (Open Secrets: real estate industry contributions totaled $371 million in the 2024 cycle), adequate supply is systematically blocked in favor of scarcity that inflates existing asset values.

The scale of the problem as of April 2026: approximately 21 million renter households (49.7% of all renter households) spend more than 30% of their income on housing — the standard "cost-burdened" threshold. Approximately 11 million are severely cost-burdened (more than 50% of income). The National Low Income Housing Coalition's 2024 Out of Reach report found that a full-time minimum-wage worker cannot afford a modest one-bedroom apartment in any state in the country, and a worker earning the median renter's wage cannot afford a two-bedroom apartment in 30 states. The 2020-2024 home price surge (53% per Redfin) has effectively foreclosed homeownership for an additional cohort of first-time buyers, concentrating housing wealth among existing owners while locking out the young and the poor.

The root cause is not mysterious: the United States has built fewer homes per capita than any OECD peer for four consecutive decades. The McKinsey Global Institute estimated in 2021 that the U.S. was short approximately 5.5 million housing units. The Pew Research Center's 2024 analysis put the shortage at 4.7 million units. The shortage is not a natural condition — it is the product of exclusionary zoning (single-family-only zones covering 75% of residential land in major U.S. cities, per the Urban Institute), environmental review processes weaponized by incumbent property owners to block new construction, and building codes that add $30,000-$100,000 per unit in compliance costs without equivalent safety benefits.

**The statutory right framework**: Federal legislation should establish housing as a statutory entitlement — not a constitutional amendment (which is a 20-year project) but a federal civil right analogous to the Fair Housing Act, with an affirmative enforceable obligation on federal, state, and local governments to ensure access to safe, stable housing. The model is Scotland's Housing (Homeless Persons) Act (1977), which established a statutory duty for local authorities to provide permanent housing to unintentionally homeless households meeting eligibility criteria. Scotland's implementation has evolved through multiple revisions, and as of 2012, Scotland provides an absolute legal right to settled accommodation for all unintentionally homeless people — enforceable by judicial review. The result: Scotland's rough sleeping rate is significantly lower than England's (which has no such right), and its rate of family homelessness is substantially lower than U.S. equivalents. Wales passed the Housing (Wales) Act 2014 with strengthened prevention and assistance duties and is developing an enforceable right framework. South Africa's Constitution (Section 26) provides a justiciable right to "access to adequate housing," with the Constitutional Court's Government of South Africa v. Grootboom (2001) establishing that this right generates a positive state obligation to take reasonable legislative and other measures within available resources — a "progressive realization" standard that does not require instant universal housing but does require measurable progress and prohibits regression.

**The production mandate**: A rights framework without a supply mandate is an unfunded entitlement. The federal statute must include:

1. A federal housing production target: 500,000 new affordable units per year for 10 years (closing approximately 50% of the current gap, after accounting for demolition and deterioration of existing stock). Funded through a Affordable Housing Production Fund capitalized at $50B/year from the land value tax revenue in Domain 5c and the offshore loophole closures in Domain 5e.

2. Zoning preemption: Federal funding for major transportation, infrastructure, and urban development conditioned on adoption of zoning codes that allow multi-family housing near transit corridors and in all residential zones within metropolitan areas — the model adopted by Minneapolis (2040 plan, which eliminated single-family-only zoning citywide) and followed by Oregon (statewide elimination of single-family-only zoning for most jurisdictions, 2019) and California (AB 2011, ADU legislation). The Minneapolis experience is particularly instructive: housing construction accelerated, rent growth slowed compared to peer cities, and the dire predictions of neighborhood destruction proved unfounded.

3. A Social Housing Development Authority, modeled on Vienna's Wiener Wohnen (the city-owned housing system that provides high-quality, mixed-income housing to 62% of Vienna's population at below-market rents) and Singapore's Housing Development Board (HDB, which provides public housing to approximately 80% of Singapore's population at subsidized prices — Singapore has achieved 91% homeownership through a combination of HDB resale rights and CPF integration). The U.S. equivalent would not require 80% public housing stock but could provide 20% of new units in markets with the highest affordability burdens, permanently removing them from speculative pressure.

4. Tenant protection floor: Federal preemption of state laws that ban rent stabilization (currently the law in 29 states), with a federal standard allowing localities to adopt rent stabilization ordinances covering buildings built before 2000 — a compromise that protects existing stock without removing incentives for new construction. The New Jersey rent stabilization model (covering pre-2010 buildings in municipalities that adopt ordinances, with Costa-Hawkins-style exemptions for new construction) provides the template.

*Fiscal impact*: Affordable Housing Production Fund at $50B/year (capitalized from land value tax and offshore loophole revenue per Domain 5). Tenant protection administration and enforcement: $500M/year. Social Housing Development Authority capitalization: $20B over 5 years (with the authority then self-financing through below-market rents that cover operating costs). Against this investment, the fiscal cost of inadequate housing is enormous and currently hidden: emergency shelter costs average $33,000/person/year in major cities; hospitalization costs for homeless individuals are 3-4x higher than housed individuals ($40,000 vs. $10,000 annually per the Journal of Urban Economics); criminal justice costs for homeless individuals are 2-3x higher. The Rand Corporation's 2020 study of Housing First in Los Angeles found that housing chronically homeless individuals cost $605/month — compared to $3,047/month for the combination of shelter, hospitalization, and criminal justice costs the same individual generated while unhoused. Housing First is cheaper than homelessness by a factor of five. At scale, adequately housing the 650,000+ currently homeless Americans at full Housing First costs would require approximately $5B/year in direct support costs — while saving an estimated $24B/year in avoided downstream costs.

---

## Domain 4: Digital Government Infrastructure

*Companion document: `domain-04-economic-policy.md` — full evidence library for economic inequality, wage stagnation, wealth concentration, financialization, and structural economic reform (productivity-pay gap, Gini trajectory, CEO-to-worker pay ratios, wealth tax design, sovereign wealth fund options, international comparisons). The main proposal treats economic policy across Domains 5, 17, 18, and 20; the companion document provides the integrated evidentiary foundation.*

### The Problem

The U.S. government operates on fragmented, aging technology infrastructure that imposes enormous costs on citizens and the economy while enabling corruption and undermining democratic accountability. The digital gap between American government and peer nations is not a matter of degree — it is a generation behind, and the consequences are measurable.

**Administrative burden as a democratic tax.** The federal government operates over 12,000 distinct IT systems, many running on legacy infrastructure decades old — the IRS's Individual Master File system was built in the 1960s on COBOL, the Social Security Administration runs critical systems on assembly language from the 1970s, and the Department of Defense failed its seventh consecutive financial audit in 2024 because its 2,300+ financial systems cannot produce consistent data. Americans spend an estimated 11.5 billion hours annually on federal paperwork (Office of Information and Regulatory Affairs). The Small Business Administration estimates that regulatory compliance costs small businesses $12,000 per employee annually — with a substantial share attributable to redundant data entry across unconnected government systems. This administrative burden is regressive: it falls hardest on low-income individuals navigating benefits systems, small businesses without compliance departments, and communities with limited English proficiency or internet access.

**The benefits gap: eligible but unenrolled.** The most direct democratic consequence of broken digital infrastructure is that tens of millions of Americans who are legally entitled to benefits do not receive them because the application process is too complex, too opaque, or too punitive. Approximately 20% of eligible SNAP recipients do not receive the benefit, 40% of eligible Medicaid recipients are not enrolled, the IRS estimates that $60-100 billion in Earned Income Tax Credits go unclaimed annually, and 50% of elderly people eligible for the Low Income Home Energy Assistance Program do not receive it — not because they don't qualify, but because the system requires them to navigate byzantine applications while in economic distress. The Census Bureau estimates that $80-100 billion in federal benefits go unclaimed each year. This is a system designed around the presumption that citizens must prove eligibility rather than one that proactively delivers benefits to those who qualify.

**Government opacity as a corruption enabler.** When government spending, contracts, and regulatory actions are distributed across thousands of incompatible systems with no standardized public access, corruption becomes structurally easier — not because officials are uniquely dishonest but because the information architecture makes detection nearly impossible. The Government Accountability Office has maintained a "High Risk" list of government programs vulnerable to fraud and waste; as of 2025, 37 programs appear on it, with an estimated $247 billion in improper payments annually. Meanwhile, federal contract data — theoretically available through USAspending.gov — is riddled with errors: a 2023 GAO audit found significant data quality issues in over 55% of the records examined, making independent oversight of $700+ billion in annual federal contracting effectively impossible.

**Digital exclusion compounds democratic exclusion.** As government services move online, the 24 million Americans without broadband access (FCC 2024 estimate; independent researchers suggest double), the 22% of seniors who do not use the internet, and the estimated 32 million adults with limited literacy face compounding exclusion. The pandemic revealed this starkly: states that implemented unemployment insurance systems requiring online-only applications saw backlogs of months; states with accessible multi-channel systems processed claims in days. Digital government that is not universally accessible does not modernize — it stratifies.

**The democratic consequence.** Citizens cannot hold a government accountable when they cannot see what it spends, who it contracts with, or how it makes decisions. Citizens cannot participate in the democratic process when navigating government services requires specialized knowledge and dozens of hours. Government that is opaque and burdensome breeds cynicism and disengagement — the same conditions that enable authoritarian populism. The 160-movement dataset shows that transparent governance is among the most effective inoculants against democratic backsliding: citizens who can see what their government does are less susceptible to narratives that the system is rigged.

### Proposed Reforms

**4a. Open government data APIs.**

Mandate that all non-personal government data be published as real-time, machine-readable APIs. Every government dataset — budget expenditures, grant awards, regulatory actions, environmental data, economic statistics — available in standardized formats that citizens, journalists, researchers, and civil society can use. Establish a Federal Data Standards Office to define and enforce interoperability requirements across all agencies, with authority to block system procurement that does not meet open-data standards.

*Precedent*: Estonia's X-Road connects 929+ institutions and enables service interoperability without a central database. It is open-source and has been adopted by Finland, Iceland, and Japan. Over 2,000 data services operate through X-Road, processing 1.5 billion queries annually — serving a country of 1.3 million. The architecture principle is decentralized interoperability: each institution maintains its own data but makes it available through standardized APIs, eliminating both centralization risk and data silos. The UK's Government Digital Service (GDS), established in 2011, redesigned government services around user needs rather than departmental structure. GDS reduced the cost of government digital services by 80-90% while dramatically improving usability — GOV.UK replaced 1,882 separate government websites with a single platform. South Korea's Government 3.0 initiative (2013) mandated proactive data disclosure across all government agencies, with over 42,000 datasets published as open APIs; independent analysis found that open government data contributed an estimated $32 billion to the South Korean economy in 2023. The U.S. DATA Act (2014) and OPEN Government Data Act (2018) established the principle but implementation has been incomplete — a 2024 GAO review found that only 40% of required datasets were published in compliant format.

*Evidence*: The McKinsey Global Institute estimates that open government data could generate $3-5 trillion in economic value globally per year across seven sectors. The Open Data Institute's analysis of 30 countries found that open data leaders (Estonia, South Korea, UK, Denmark) consistently outperform peers on corruption perceptions, government effectiveness, and business formation rates. Within the U.S., the cities with the most mature open data programs (New York, Chicago, San Francisco) have seen measurable improvements in government accountability, civic technology innovation, and research utility.

*Implementation pathway*: Executive order plus strengthened statutory mandates. The technical infrastructure exists; the barrier is bureaucratic inertia and agency resistance to sharing data. Estimated timeline: 2 years for priority datasets (budget, contracts, grants, environmental monitoring), 5 years for comprehensive coverage.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal Data Standards Office and API infrastructure: $300-500M/year. This is offset many times over: eliminating data incompatibility between federal agencies saves an estimated $2-4B/year in redundant data collection and reconciliation (GAO estimate). The economic value of open government data to the private sector, researchers, and civic organizations — estimated at $100-200B/year for the U.S. (extrapolating from EU and South Korean analyses) — dwarfs the investment.

**4b. Digital identity with privacy protections.**

Pilot a national digital identity system based on W3C Decentralized Identifier (DID) standards, with selective disclosure (prove you are over 18 without revealing your birthdate; prove citizenship without revealing your address). The system must have offline and in-person fallbacks for the 13% of Americans who lack home broadband and the 22% of seniors who do not use the internet. The system must be operated by an independent authority with constitutionally modeled protections — not a cabinet department that changes with administrations.

*Precedent*: Estonia has operated eID for 25+ years with no major security failures. 99% of public services are available online (only marriage, divorce, and real estate transactions require in-person interaction). Citizens save an estimated 820 years of working time annually through digital services — in a country of 1.3 million. The EU is rolling out EUDI Wallet under eIDAS 2.0, with a target of 80% citizen adoption by 2030 across 27 member states — the largest coordinated digital identity deployment in history. India's Aadhaar system has enrolled 1.3 billion people and enabled direct benefit transfers of over $300 billion, dramatically reducing fraud and leakage in welfare programs — though it also illustrates the surveillance risks of centralized biometric identity without adequate privacy protections. Singapore's SingPass, serving 5.5 million residents, demonstrates a middle path: strong identity verification with granular consent controls and no centralized biometric database. The architecture principle is identity without surveillance — the system proves claims without creating a centralized database of citizen activity.

*The April 2026 constraint*: The voting technology research (`remote-electronic-voting-research.md`, Section 10) identifies digital identity as the critical enabler for long-term voting infrastructure improvements, but also demonstrates that the current political environment has weaponized exactly this kind of infrastructure. DOGE's access to SSA data — including the NUMIDENT database containing identity records for nearly every living American — and the subsequent sharing of that data with a political advocacy group illustrate the risk: a digital identity system built without constitutional firewalls from enforcement agencies becomes a surveillance and suppression tool. The design must ensure that the identity system is operated by an independent body with protected tenure, subject to judicial review at every data-sharing point, and structurally unable to share identity verification data with immigration enforcement, law enforcement, or political operations. This governance architecture does not currently exist in any U.S. proposal, and designing it is a harder problem than the technology. Estonia's e-ID works because it serves all citizens equally and is not entangled with enforcement; a U.S. equivalent would inherit the political toxicity of immigration enforcement, voter ID laws, and the citizenship question unless its governance is designed from the start to prevent that.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal pilot program (statute). Voluntary adoption initially, expanding as services demonstrate value. 5-10 year timeline for core infrastructure. Must be preceded by or concurrent with the independent agency design described above — the technology without the governance is a liability, not an asset.

*Fiscal impact*: Digital identity infrastructure development and pilot: $2-4B over 5 years. Annual operating cost at scale: $500M-1B/year. The return is substantial: identity fraud costs the U.S. economy an estimated $56 billion annually (Javelin Strategy, 2023); the IRS estimated $5.7 billion in identity-theft-related tax fraud in FY2023 alone. Streamlined identity verification across government services would save an estimated $10-15B/year in duplicated verification costs, fraud prevention, and reduced improper payments. Estonia's digital identity system generates estimated savings of 2% of GDP annually — equivalent to $500B/year if replicated at U.S. scale, though significant adjustments for scale and complexity would be required.

**4c. Transparent public finance ledger.**

Every government transaction — tax collection, transfer payment, contract award, salary — published to a public, searchable, machine-readable ledger within 24 hours. Not a blockchain (too complex, too slow); an append-only, cryptographically signed log with independent audit. The ledger should be searchable by vendor, agency, program, and geographic area, with anomaly detection that flags unusual patterns for public review.

*Evidence*: The from-scratch-design research identifies transparent public finance as a cross-cutting anti-corruption mechanism that works regardless of governance model. When every transaction is publicly logged and searchable, corruption requires not just dishonesty but the ability to alter a cryptographically secured record visible to millions of observers. The current U.S. system is the opposite: $247 billion in annual improper payments, $700+ billion in federal contracts with unreliable public data, and a Department of Defense that has never passed a financial audit despite spending $886 billion annually.

*Precedent*: Brazil's Portal da Transparência (Transparency Portal), established in 2004, publishes every federal expenditure in real time and is searchable by any citizen. The portal is credited with a measurable reduction in corruption — municipalities subject to random federal audits (and whose results were published) experienced a 20% reduction in corruption-related irregularities compared to non-audited municipalities (Ferraz and Finan, 2008 — one of the most cited studies in anti-corruption economics). Georgia (the country) implemented a similar real-time expenditure transparency system during its 2003-2012 anti-corruption reforms, contributing to its improvement from 124th to 51st on Transparency International's Corruption Perceptions Index — the most dramatic anti-corruption success story of the 21st century. South Korea's e-procurement system (KONEPS) handles $110 billion in annual government procurement through a single transparent platform, with open competition and automatic publication of all contract terms — reducing procurement corruption to near-zero. The UK's Whole of Government Accounts publish consolidated financial statements for all of central government, including every entity and transaction — the most comprehensive government financial transparency in any major economy.

*Implementation pathway*: Executive order for federal agencies, statute for comprehensive mandate. The technical challenge is standardization across thousands of legacy financial systems — the DATA Act laid groundwork but implementation remains incomplete. Timeline: 3 years for top 20 agencies (covering 90%+ of expenditure), 7 years for comprehensive coverage.

*Fiscal impact*: Ledger infrastructure development and maintenance: $200-400M/year. The return is enormous: reducing improper payments by even 10% (a conservative estimate given the Brazilian and Georgian evidence) saves $25 billion annually. Independent academic analysis of Brazil's transparency portal estimated a return of $33 for every $1 invested in transparency infrastructure. GAO's existing work on improper payments suggests that better data visibility alone — before any enforcement action — reduces error rates by 15-25%.

**4d. Automated service delivery.**

Implement the "once only" principle: government cannot ask citizens for information it already holds. If you register a new business, you are not asked for your name, address, and tax number — the system already has them. Proactively identify benefit eligibility and deliver benefits without requiring application. Establish a Federal Digital Services Agency (modeled on GDS and Estonia's Information System Authority) with authority to redesign cross-agency service delivery around citizen needs rather than departmental structures.

*Evidence*: The benefits gap is the clearest evidence of system failure: approximately 20% of eligible SNAP recipients do not receive the benefit, 40% of eligible Medicaid recipients are not enrolled, $60-100 billion in EITCs go unclaimed annually, and 50% of elderly people eligible for LIHEAP do not receive it — not because they don't qualify, but because the system requires them to navigate complex applications while in economic distress. The Census Bureau estimates that $80-100 billion in federal benefits go unclaimed each year. Code for America's GetCalFresh simplified California's SNAP application from 45 minutes to 10 minutes and increased completion rates by 51% — demonstrating that the barrier is design, not eligibility.

*Precedent*: Denmark's child benefit is paid automatically based on birth registration. No form required. The Borger.dk portal provides a single point of access for all government services, with pre-populated forms using data the government already holds. Estonia's "once only" principle means that no government agency can ask for information that another agency already has — a legal prohibition, not just a design aspiration. As a result, 99% of government services are available online, and citizens spend an average of 5 minutes on interactions that take hours in the U.S. Australia's myGov platform connects 15 federal services through a single digital identity, with automatic eligibility assessment for multiple programs simultaneously. Finland's Kela (social insurance institution) proactively identifies benefit eligibility and contacts citizens who may qualify — reversing the burden from citizen-must-apply to government-must-deliver.

*Implementation pathway*: Executive and regulatory action for the "once only" principle within existing federal agencies. Statute for cross-agency data sharing and the Federal Digital Services Agency. 10-15 year timeline for full service migration. Phased approach: begin with the highest-impact, highest-volume services (tax filing, benefits enrollment, business registration) and expand systematically.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal Digital Services Agency and service redesign: $1-2B/year. The return: closing even half the benefits gap would deliver $40-50 billion annually to eligible citizens who currently go unserved. Administrative savings from eliminating redundant data collection across agencies: $5-10B/year (based on the UK's GDS experience of 80-90% cost reduction applied to a fraction of U.S. government digital services). Reduced improper payments from better data sharing: $10-20B/year. Total estimated return: $55-80B/year against a $1-2B annual investment — a 30-50x ROI comparable to inspector general oversight (Domain 2c).

---

## Domain 5: Fiscal Reform

### The Problem

The U.S. fiscal system is broken in ways that compound every other structural problem in this proposal. The tax code is simultaneously too complex for ordinary citizens to navigate, too full of loopholes for the wealthy to feel, and too poorly enforced to collect what is owed. The result is a system that redistributes from working people to capital owners, from the transparent to the opaque, and from the compliant to the evasive — while generating enormous deadweight costs that serve no one.

**Compliance burden as regressive tax.** The U.S. tax code runs to 6,871 pages plus approximately 70,000 pages of regulations, rulings, and guidance. Americans spend an estimated 6.5 billion hours and $400 billion per year on tax compliance (National Taxpayers Union Foundation, 2024) — more than the GDP of 80% of the world's countries. This is pure economic waste: it produces no goods, no services, no public benefit. The burden is profoundly regressive: wealthy individuals and corporations hire specialists to exploit complexity; middle-income taxpayers bear the highest compliance costs relative to income; low-income filers who are entitled to refundable credits (EITC, Child Tax Credit) face the most complex filing requirements and the highest audit rates. The IRS audits EITC recipients at roughly the same rate as taxpayers earning over $500,000 — despite the $600-700 billion annual "tax gap" being overwhelmingly concentrated among high-income earners.

**The tax gap: $600-700 billion in annual evasion.** The IRS estimates that the gross tax gap — the difference between what is owed and what is voluntarily paid — exceeded $688 billion for tax year 2021, with an estimated net gap (after enforcement and late payments) of $606 billion. The distribution is stark: the top 1% of earners fail to report approximately 21% of their income (IRS/Guyton et al., 2021), accounting for roughly $175 billion annually in unpaid taxes. Wage earners, whose income is reported by employers and subject to withholding, have a compliance rate above 99%. Income from capital — partnerships, S corporations, sole proprietorships, foreign accounts — has compliance rates between 45% and 83%. The tax system effectively operates on an honor system for the wealthy and a mandatory system for workers. This is not a law enforcement problem; it is a structural design problem.

**Regressive in practice despite progressive in theory.** The federal income tax has nominal progressive rates, but the effective tax system — including payroll taxes, state and local taxes, and the preferential treatment of capital income — is far less progressive than it appears, and for the very wealthiest, it is regressive. ProPublica's 2021 analysis of leaked IRS data revealed that the 25 wealthiest Americans paid a "true tax rate" of 3.4% on their wealth gains from 2014-2018, compared to the approximately 14% effective federal income tax rate paid by median-income Americans. The mechanism is the "buy, borrow, die" strategy: accumulate wealth in appreciating assets (taxed at 0% until sold), borrow against those assets for living expenses (interest is deductible, loans are not income), and die (heirs receive a stepped-up basis, erasing the unrealized gains permanently). Warren Buffett's observation that he pays a lower effective tax rate than his secretary is not an anomaly — it is the system working as designed.

**Corporate tax erosion.** Federal corporate tax revenue as a share of GDP has declined from approximately 4% in the 1960s to 1-2% in recent years. The 2017 Tax Cuts and Jobs Act reduced the statutory rate from 35% to 21% and introduced provisions allowing immediate expensing and offshore profit shifting that further reduced effective rates. A 2024 Government Accountability Office analysis found that profitable large corporations paid an average effective federal tax rate of 9% — less than half the statutory rate. Meanwhile, 55 of the largest U.S. corporations paid zero federal income tax on $40 billion in combined profits in at least one recent year (ITEP, 2021). The OECD's 15% global minimum corporate tax (Pillar Two, effective 2024) is a step forward but is designed as a floor, not a solution — and U.S. implementation remains incomplete.

**Wealth concentration as a democratic threat.** The fiscal system both reflects and accelerates wealth concentration that is incompatible with democratic governance. The top 1% of Americans hold 32% of total wealth; the bottom 50% hold 2.6% (Federal Reserve, Q4 2024). This concentration has accelerated: the top 1% wealth share has increased from 24% in 1990 to 32% in 2024. The political science evidence is clear: Martin Gilens and Benjamin Page's 2014 study found that policy outcomes in the U.S. correlate strongly with economic elite preferences and show no statistically significant correlation with median voter preferences. A fiscal system that enables unlimited wealth accumulation while undertaxing capital is not merely an economic policy choice — it is a structural threat to democratic self-governance, as the from-scratch-design research documents across every governance model studied.

**The democratic consequence.** A fiscal system that is incomprehensible to ordinary citizens, that taxes labor more heavily than capital, that permits the wealthiest to pay lower effective rates than middle-income workers, and that fails to collect $600+ billion annually in owed taxes cannot sustain democratic legitimacy. Citizens who perceive the tax system as rigged are correct — and their rational cynicism erodes support for the public investment that every other domain in this proposal requires. Fiscal reform is not a separate domain. It is the funding mechanism for democratic renewal.

### Proposed Reforms

**5a. Pre-populated tax returns (immediate).**

The IRS already has the data — employer wage reports, bank interest statements, broker 1099s — to compute most citizens' tax obligations. Pre-populate returns and send them to citizens for review and approval, as Sweden, Denmark, Estonia, and the UK already do. For approximately 85-90% of citizens, the annual "filing" becomes a 5-minute review of a pre-computed statement.

*Evidence*: Sweden's Skatteverket pre-fills most returns using employer and bank data. 74% of Swedes file by SMS or app. Average time: 3 minutes. Estonia achieves 98% digital filing with an average completion time of 3-5 minutes. The UK's PAYE system means approximately 80% of workers never file a return at all. Japan's year-end adjustment system (nenmatsu chōsei) has employers handle most tax calculations for salaried workers — approximately 80% of workers never file a return. Denmark's pre-filled returns have a 99% acceptance rate, with citizens simply confirming the pre-computed amount. South Korea's Hometax system pre-populates returns using data from 40+ reporting institutions, achieving near-universal digital filing. The IRS's own Direct File pilot (2024) demonstrated that the technical capability exists — 140,000 taxpayers in 12 states used it successfully in its first year. (See `from-scratch-design.md`, Model T2.)

*Implementation pathway*: The IRS has the legal authority and the data. The barrier is political opposition from the tax preparation industry (H&R Block, Intuit/TurboTax), which has actively lobbied Congress to prevent the IRS from offering free filing — spending over $39 million on lobbying between 2006 and 2023 (OpenSecrets). This is a concentrated-benefits-vs-diffuse-costs problem: 300,000 industry employees are intensely motivated to prevent the reform; 150 million taxpayers each save a few hours. Overcoming this requires political will, not technical innovation.

*Fiscal impact*: IRS system development for pre-populated returns: $500M-1B one-time investment, $100-200M/year ongoing. The return: American taxpayers would save an estimated 4-5 billion hours annually and $200-300 billion in compliance costs (approximately 50-75% of total compliance burden falls on returns that could be pre-populated). IRS processing costs would decrease as error rates decline — pre-populated returns in Sweden and Denmark have error rates under 2%, compared to approximately 21% for self-prepared U.S. returns. The compliance cost savings alone represent a 200-300x return on the technology investment.

**5b. Simplified tax code: all income is income.**

Eliminate the distinction between wages, dividends, capital gains, carried interest, and other income sources. Apply a single progressive rate schedule to all income, including unrealized gains at death (eliminating the stepped-up basis). Eliminate most deductions except: basic personal exemption, refundable childcare credit, refundable healthcare credit, refundable education credit, and charitable giving deduction capped at 25% of income. Tax unrealized capital gains above $100 million annually (targeting the "buy, borrow, die" strategy directly).

This directly addresses the "buy, borrow, die" avoidance strategy documented by ProPublica, where billionaires borrow against appreciated assets and pay effective federal income tax rates under 3%. The distinction between "ordinary income" and "capital gains" has no economic justification — a dollar earned from labor is identical to a dollar earned from investment. The preferential rate exists because capital owners have disproportionate political influence, not because of any efficiency argument that survives scrutiny.

*Evidence*: The Congressional Research Service has consistently found that the preferential capital gains rate costs approximately $130 billion annually in foregone revenue, with over 75% of the benefit accruing to the top 1% of earners. The Tax Policy Center estimates that eliminating the stepped-up basis alone would raise $40-50 billion annually. Australia taxes capital gains as ordinary income (with a 50% discount for assets held over one year) without measurable negative effects on investment — the discount provides an incentive for long-term holding while ensuring capital gains are still taxed at meaningful rates. Denmark taxes capital gains and dividends at rates nearly identical to wage income (up to 42%). Norway's system taxes labor and capital at similar effective rates through its dual income tax structure.

*Precedent*: New Zealand does not have a separate capital gains tax — most capital gains are untaxed, but the country has explored comprehensive taxation and its experience illustrates the political dynamics. The more relevant precedents are countries that tax capital gains at or near ordinary income rates: Denmark, Australia (with discount), Finland, and Norway. The U.S. itself taxed capital gains at the same rate as ordinary income from 1986-1990 following the Tax Reform Act of 1986 — the most comprehensive tax simplification in modern American history, passed with bipartisan support under Reagan, which demonstrated that unified rate treatment is economically and politically feasible.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. Revenue-neutral version (lower all rates while broadening the base) has precedent in the 1986 Tax Reform Act and may build bipartisan support. Revenue-positive version funds the public investments throughout this proposal. The primary political obstacle is the financial services industry, which profits from the complexity that distinct income categories create.

*Fiscal impact*: Equalizing capital gains and ordinary income rates: $130-180B/year in additional revenue. Eliminating stepped-up basis: $40-50B/year. Unrealized gains tax on holdings above $100M: $50-80B/year (Treasury estimate for the Biden administration's proposed billionaire minimum tax). Deduction simplification: $100-200B/year from closing loopholes that disproportionately benefit high earners. Total revenue potential: $320-510B/year — enough to fund a substantial share of the public investments across all 22 domains.

**5c. Land value tax pilot.**

Implement a land value tax at 1-2% on commercial and high-value residential land, phased in over 10-15 years with transition credits for current owners. LVT is the least economically distorting tax — because land supply is fixed, taxing it does not reduce supply or discourage productive activity. It is also the most evasion-resistant: land cannot be hidden offshore or concealed in shell companies. LVT encourages productive use of land (penalizing speculation and vacant lot banking) while reducing the tax burden on improvements (which discourages construction under the current property tax system).

*Evidence*: LVT is currently implemented in Denmark (grundskyld, land value tax for municipalities), Estonia (a 0.1-2.5% land tax that is the primary local revenue source), Hong Kong (government land lease system generating approximately 15% of government revenue), Taiwan (a dual land value tax and land value increment tax), Singapore (leasehold system where the government owns 90% of land), and parts of Australia and the U.S. (Pennsylvania's split-rate tax in Pittsburgh and Harrisburg). Pennsylvania's evidence is particularly relevant: Harrisburg adopted a split-rate tax in 1975 (taxing land at 6x the rate of improvements), and over the following 25 years, the number of vacant structures in the city fell from 4,200 to fewer than 500, while building permits increased substantially. The tax has cross-ideological support: Milton Friedman called it "the least bad tax"; Joseph Stiglitz proved the "Henry George Theorem" showing that land value taxation is sufficient to fund all public goods in a spatial economy; William Vickrey called it "the least bad tax." The IMF has recommended LVT as an efficient revenue source for developing and developed countries alike. (See `from-scratch-design.md`, Model T1.)

*Precedent*: Denmark's land value tax system is the most relevant large-country precedent. Danish municipalities levy a land value tax (grundskyld) at rates set locally (typically 1.6-3.4%), assessed on land value only (not improvements). The system has operated since 1926 and is credited with encouraging efficient land use and preventing the worst excesses of land speculation seen in countries without such taxes. Estonia's land tax (maamaks), introduced in 1993, ranges from 0.1-2.5% and is the primary revenue source for local governments — contributing to Estonia's consistently high rankings for economic freedom and low corruption.

*Implementation pathway*: Municipal and state pilots first. Pennsylvania cities provide the existing U.S. model. Federal incentives (matching grants for states that adopt split-rate property taxes) can accelerate adoption. A federal LVT on commercial land above $5M in assessed value could be implemented by statute and would primarily affect large landholders, real estate investment trusts, and speculative land banks.

*Fiscal impact*: A 1% federal LVT on commercial and high-value residential land (above $2M per parcel) would generate an estimated $100-200B/year in revenue (based on total U.S. land value of approximately $25-30 trillion, with a substantial fraction in commercial and high-value residential parcels). Municipal LVT adoption would reduce local reliance on property taxes that penalize construction, with estimated efficiency gains of $20-40B/year from reduced speculation and increased productive land use. The transition cost (credits for current owners to smooth phase-in) would reduce initial revenue by 30-50% during the 10-15 year phase-in period.

**5d. Carbon tax with citizen dividend.**

Tax CO2 emissions at $100/ton, with 100% of revenue returned as an equal per-person citizen dividend ($500-700 per person annually at initial rates). Revenue-neutral for the median citizen; progressive because the wealthy emit more but receive the same dividend. The price signal internalizes the externality of carbon emissions — currently estimated at $51/ton by the EPA's social cost of carbon, though recent research suggests $185/ton or higher (Rennert et al., Nature, 2022).

*Evidence*: British Columbia's carbon tax (operational 2008-present) reduced emissions 5-15% compared to the rest of Canada (Metcalf, 2019; Murray and Rivers, 2015) without harming economic performance — BC's GDP growth matched or exceeded the Canadian average during the tax period. Sweden's carbon tax, at approximately $130/ton (the world's highest), has been operational since 1991 and is credited with reducing Swedish emissions by 27% while the economy grew 83% — the strongest evidence that carbon pricing and economic growth are compatible. The EU Emissions Trading System (EU ETS), covering approximately 40% of EU emissions, has reduced covered emissions by 43% since 2005. Switzerland's CO2 levy ($130/ton) returns two-thirds to citizens and businesses through reduced health insurance premiums. The carbon tax with dividend model has cross-ideological support — the Climate Leadership Council, backed by former Republican secretaries of state and treasury (George Shultz, James Baker), proposed this structure.

*Precedent*: Canada's federal carbon pricing backstop (implemented 2019) is the closest large-country precedent to the proposed dividend model: the federal government sets a price floor ($80 CAD/ton in 2024, rising to $170 CAD/ton by 2030), and in provinces without their own pricing system, 90% of revenue is returned to households as the Canada Carbon Rebate — making the bottom 60% of households net beneficiaries. The dividend structure proved politically durable despite opposition, surviving a change in government at the provincial level in multiple provinces.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. No constitutional barrier. The dividend mechanism is critical for political durability — the Alaska Permanent Fund (oil revenue dividend since 1982, averaging $1,600/person/year) demonstrates that citizen dividends create a powerful constituency for their own continuation. A carbon tax with dividend should be designed so that the majority of households receive more in dividends than they pay in higher energy costs — as the Canadian and Swiss models achieve.

*Fiscal impact*: At $100/ton on approximately 5 billion metric tons of U.S. CO2 emissions, gross revenue would be approximately $500B/year. With 100% returned as citizen dividend: approximately $1,500 per person ($6,000 for a family of four) at initial rates. As emissions decline (the intended effect), revenue and dividends decline proportionally — but the economic benefit of avoided climate damage ($185/ton social cost × emissions reduced) far exceeds the declining revenue. The economic cost of carbon pricing is estimated at 0.1-0.5% of GDP (Metcalf, 2019) — a fraction of the estimated 2-10% of GDP in annual climate damage costs by 2050 under a no-pricing scenario (Hsiang et al., Science, 2017).

**5e. Close offshore loopholes: extend global financial reporting.**

Extend FATCA-style reporting requirements globally, coordinated with the OECD global minimum corporate tax framework (Pillar Two). Require mandatory financial account reporting from all institutions managing assets of U.S. taxpayers, including beneficial ownership disclosure for all entities. Implement the Corporate Transparency Act's beneficial ownership registry with adequate enforcement resources and extend it to trusts and other vehicles commonly used for tax avoidance. Establish automatic information exchange with all jurisdictions (expanding beyond FATCA's existing 113 partner countries) and impose withholding penalties on financial flows to non-cooperating jurisdictions.

*Evidence*: The Tax Justice Network estimates that $427 billion in tax revenue is lost globally to offshore tax abuse annually, with the U.S. losing approximately $60 billion. The Panama Papers (2016) and Pandora Papers (2021) revealed the scale of offshore tax evasion and avoidance by wealthy individuals and corporations — involving over 14,000 entities in the Panama Papers alone and 35 current and former world leaders in the Pandora Papers. FATCA, enacted in 2010, demonstrated that mandatory reporting requirements are effective: voluntary compliance by U.S. taxpayers with offshore accounts increased significantly after FATCA implementation, and over 100,000 taxpayers disclosed previously unreported offshore accounts through associated amnesty programs.

*Precedent*: The OECD's Common Reporting Standard (CRS), modeled on FATCA, now operates across 100+ jurisdictions and enables automatic exchange of financial account information. The EU's Anti-Tax Avoidance Directives (ATAD I and II) establish minimum anti-avoidance standards across all member states, including controlled foreign corporation rules, interest limitation rules, and anti-hybrid mismatch provisions. Denmark's aggressive pursuit of offshore tax evasion — including purchasing leaked banking data (Panama Papers) and using it for enforcement — recovered revenues far exceeding the acquisition cost and demonstrated political feasibility. Australia's Multinational Anti-Avoidance Law (MAAL) and Diverted Profits Tax target profit shifting by multinationals, with the ATO collecting an additional AUD $8.6 billion from multinationals in 2022-23 through enhanced enforcement.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute for beneficial ownership disclosure expansion. Treasury regulation for expanded FATCA enforcement. Coordination with OECD Pillar Two implementation (currently incomplete in U.S. law — the Undertaxed Profits Rule has not been enacted). The primary political obstacle is the financial services industry in states like Delaware, Nevada, and South Dakota that profit from opacity.

*Fiscal impact*: Full implementation of global reporting, beneficial ownership disclosure, and anti-avoidance measures: estimated $60-100B/year in recovered revenue from offshore tax evasion and avoidance. IRS enforcement funding (the IRA allocated $80B over 10 years, much of which has been clawed back) is the binding constraint: every additional $1 in IRS enforcement spending returns $5-12 in collected revenue (CBO/Treasury estimates). The proposed reforms require sustained IRS enforcement funding of $15-20B/year (compared to the current $12.3B) — an investment with the highest fiscal return of any government spending.

---

## Domain 6: Judicial Independence and Rule of Law

### The Problem

The federal judiciary has been the most effective brake on the most extreme executive actions — 358 lawsuits, 700+ favorable rulings as of March 2026 (documented in `litigation-tracker-2026.md`). But this shield is eroding. The Supreme Court's 6-3 conservative supermajority has curtailed universal injunctions, granted presidential immunity, weakened agency authority, and is not a neutral referee. The administration has begun openly defying court orders — testing whether courts can enforce their rulings without executive cooperation. If this precedent is established, the constitutional order effectively collapses.

### Proposed Reforms

**6a. Supreme Court term limits.**

Replace life tenure with 18-year non-renewable terms, with one vacancy occurring every two years. This is the single most widely supported court reform among legal scholars across the ideological spectrum. It depoliticizes individual appointments (every president gets the same number), reduces the stakes of any single vacancy, ensures the court reflects evolving legal thought, and eliminates the incentive to appoint young ideologues who will serve for decades.

*Precedent*: Every major democracy except the U.S. has term limits or mandatory retirement ages for its highest court. Germany's Federal Constitutional Court uses 12-year non-renewable terms. The from-scratch-design research recommends 15-year non-renewable terms for the constitutional court, with merit selection by an independent judicial appointments commission.

*Implementation pathway*: There is a credible legal argument that term limits can be implemented by statute (justices rotate to senior status on lower courts after 18 years, retaining their Article III commission). The alternative is constitutional amendment. The Presidential Commission on the Supreme Court (2021) found term limits to be the reform with the broadest scholarly support.

**6b. Enforcement mechanisms for court orders.**

Establish statutory mechanisms for enforcing court orders against the executive branch. Options include: automatic appropriation freezes for agencies in contempt of court, mandatory special counsel appointment when the executive defies a court order, and state attorney general standing to enforce federal court orders when the federal executive will not.

*Evidence*: The litigation tracker documents multiple cases where the administration has defied court orders — in immigration enforcement (CASA v. DHS, Ramirez Ovando v. Noem), in facility construction (Maryland v. Noem), and in data privacy. The existing enforcement mechanism depends on executive cooperation, creating a circular dependency that a defiant executive can exploit.

**6c. Judicial ethics enforcement.**

Establish an independent inspector general for the federal judiciary with authority to investigate ethics complaints, require financial disclosure, and refer findings for disciplinary action. Current judicial ethics enforcement is self-policing — a structural conflict of interest.

**6d. Court expansion.**

Expand the lower federal courts to match caseload (which has tripled since the last major expansion in 1990). For the Supreme Court, expansion is a political lever: the threat of expansion may be necessary to check a supermajority that is functionally unaccountable.

**6e. Emergency docket (shadow docket) reform.**

The Supreme Court's emergency docket — colloquially the "shadow docket" — has become a primary mechanism for executive power consolidation. Historically reserved for genuinely urgent procedural matters, it has been transformed into a substantive decision-making channel where the Court grants or denies emergency relief on contested constitutional questions without full briefing, oral argument, or written opinions.

The pattern documented in the litigation tracker is structurally corrosive: the administration obtains a stay or emergency relief on a thin factual record; the lower courts treat the emergency order as binding precedent on the merits; and when the factual predicate underlying the emergency order is later discredited (as in AFSCME v. SSA, where the government was found to have provided "patently false information" to the district court), there is no mechanism for lower courts to revisit the emergency posture. The result is a one-way ratchet: emergency relief that favors executive power is locked in, and adverse factual developments are treated as irrelevant to the posture question.

Reforms:
- **Transparency**: Require written opinions for all emergency orders that alter the status quo, with identification of the authoring justice and any dissents. No more unsigned, unexplained orders on contested constitutional questions.
- **Procedural floors**: Mandate minimum briefing schedules (7 days) for emergency applications that seek to stay lower-court injunctions, except in cases of genuine physical emergency (imminent execution, natural disaster).
- **Factual record reassessment**: Establish by statute that lower courts retain authority to reassess emergency postures when the factual record materially changes after the emergency order — specifically including cases where the government is found to have provided inaccurate information in the original emergency proceedings.
- **Volume limits**: Require the Court to report annually on the volume and disposition of emergency applications by party, to make the pattern visible. Justice Sotomayor's observation about "unprecedented" emergency docket volume points at a structural shift that is not being tracked systematically.

*Evidence*: Between January 2025 and April 2026, the administration filed more emergency applications to the Supreme Court than any administration in the Court's history. Multiple stays have been granted on records that were later shown to be materially incomplete or inaccurate. The Fourth Circuit's AFSCME v. SSA ruling — where the majority treated the Supreme Court's interim stay as dispositive despite the DOJ's acknowledged submission of false information — is the clearest demonstration of the one-way ratchet.

*Implementation pathway*: Congressional statute regulating Supreme Court procedure (Congress has this authority under Article III, Section 2). The Judiciary Act has been amended many times. The political obstacle is that any reform will be framed as an attack on judicial independence by those who benefit from the current arrangement — but the reform actually restores judicial deliberation by requiring the Court to do its job (full briefing, written opinions) rather than governing by fiat.

**6f. Court order enforcement architecture.**

The current enforcement mechanism for federal court orders depends entirely on executive branch cooperation — creating a circular dependency that a defiant executive exploits. The litigation tracker documents at least four cases (CASA v. DHS, Ramirez Ovando v. Noem, Maryland v. Noem, and the DOGE data access cases) where the administration has defied, circumvented, or slow-walked compliance with federal court orders.

Reforms:
- **Automatic fiscal consequences**: When a federal court finds an agency in contempt of a court order, automatic appropriation sequestration for the non-compliant agency's discretionary budget (1% per day of non-compliance, capped at 25%), enforceable through the Treasury without requiring executive cooperation.
- **Independent compliance monitors**: Federal courts authorized to appoint independent compliance monitors (with subpoena power and access to agency records) when the government is found to have violated a court order or provided false information to the court.
- **State attorney general enforcement standing**: Grant state attorneys general standing to enforce federal court orders against federal agencies operating within their jurisdictions, when the federal executive refuses to comply.
- **Special counsel trigger**: Mandatory appointment of a special counsel (selected by a three-judge panel, not the Attorney General) when a federal court finds that the executive branch has provided materially false information in litigation.

*Evidence*: The Ramirez Ovando compliance hearings revealed that ICE agents received contradictory guidance about whether to follow federal court orders, and that no internal accountability mechanism existed for non-compliance. The AFSCME v. SSA factual record includes the DOJ's own acknowledgment that defendants provided "patently false information to the district court." These are not isolated incidents — they are symptoms of a structural failure in enforcement architecture.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. The automatic fiscal consequences mechanism is the most powerful and the most politically achievable, because it frames non-compliance as fiscal irresponsibility rather than as a partisan question. The compliance monitor mechanism already exists in limited form (consent decrees in police reform cases) and can be extended by statute.

*Overall implementation pathway for Domain 6*: All court reforms except life-tenure elimination can be enacted by statute. The political barrier is significant — court reform is coded as partisan. The path forward requires framing these reforms as structural, not ideological, and building bipartisan support through the term-limits entry point, which has the broadest appeal. Emergency docket reform and court order enforcement are the most time-sensitive proposals — the structural damage from the current emergency docket pattern compounds with each term of the Court.

**6g. Life after universal injunctions: what the Trump v. CASA ruling changed and what it demands of reform.**

On June 27, 2025, the Supreme Court issued its 6–3 decision in Trump v. CASA, Inc., written by Justice Amy Coney Barrett, holding that federal district courts lack statutory authority under the Judiciary Act of 1789 to issue "universal" or "nationwide" injunctions — orders that protect parties beyond the named plaintiffs in a specific case. This ruling fundamentally altered the landscape of constitutional litigation against executive action and demands a specific response in the reform architecture.

**What changed.** Under pre-CASA doctrine, a single district court ruling could block an unconstitutional executive action nationwide — preventing implementation while the case worked through the appeals process. This was what produced the 700+ favorable court rulings the litigation tracker documented through March 2026: each ruling applied to everyone affected, not only the named plaintiff. Post-CASA, a court can still enjoin the government as to the named plaintiffs and certified class members, but cannot extend relief to the universe of affected persons unless they join the lawsuit or a class is certified.

The immediate practical consequences are already visible in the litigation tracker. In the birthright citizenship litigation (Trump v. Barbara, argued April 1, 2026), the Supreme Court's post-CASA framework means that even if the Court rules for the plaintiffs on the merits, the injunction will bind only those parties — meaning the administration could, in theory, continue applying the unconstitutional executive order against anyone not part of the litigation, until each individual independently sues or the Supreme Court issues a ruling on the merits broad enough to function as a de facto universal standard. The SCOTUSblog analysis (April 2026) found that the majority of the justices appeared likely to rule against the administration on birthright citizenship — but the posture question under CASA looms over how effectively even a favorable ruling can be implemented.

**The reform response has four components.**

First, Congress should restore universal injunction authority by statute, explicitly codifying that district courts may issue injunctive relief "to all persons similarly situated" in cases involving violations of constitutional rights or federal statutory protections. This is a straightforward statutory enactment that does not require constitutional amendment — the Barrett majority in CASA located the prohibition in the Judiciary Act's silence, not in Article III itself, leaving room for Congress to speak expressly. A Democratic Congress should make this a first-act priority.

Second, the class action mechanism must be fortified as the alternative path when universal injunctions are unavailable. The Federal Rules of Civil Procedure's Rule 23 class certification process is the primary substitute — but it is slow (class certification typically takes 12–18 months), uncertain, and requires courts to identify manageable class definitions for what are often diffuse constitutional injuries. Congress should expedite class certification for cases involving systematic unconstitutional executive action: mandatory 60-day decision timelines on class certification motions in cases challenging federal executive policies on constitutional grounds, with automatic appellate review of certification denials on an expedited schedule.

Third, the state attorney general enforcement mechanism proposed in 6f becomes even more critical post-CASA. State attorneys general, under parens patriae doctrine, can sue on behalf of their residents as a group — achieving something close to universal injunction scope for their state's population without requiring federal class certification. The reform should codify this standing explicitly: in any case where a federal executive action affects a defined class of residents within a state, the state's attorney general has automatic statutory standing to seek injunctive relief on behalf of that class, without needing to demonstrate the individual harm required for private plaintiff standing.

Fourth, the mutual reinforcement between CASA (limiting judicial remedies) and presidential immunity (limiting executive accountability) creates a combined effect worse than either ruling alone: the executive cannot be criminally prosecuted for official acts (immunity), and courts cannot enjoin those acts as to non-parties (CASA). The constitutional repair program must address both simultaneously — which is the argument for the constitutional amendment strategies that can restore both judicially enforceable limits on executive power and the courts' traditional remedial authority.

**6h. The Supreme Court term limits implementation design: statutory versus constitutional and the sequencing question.**

The 119th Congress (2025–2026) has seen multiple term limits proposals introduced: H.R.1074 (the Supreme Court Term Limits and Regular Appointments Act, introduced by Representatives Khanna and Beyer in February 2025), the Whitehouse-Booker-Blumenthal-Padilla Biennial Appointments and Term Limits Act in the Senate, and a constitutional amendment by Senators Welch and Manchin. The Brennan Center's public opinion research shows 73% of Americans support term limits, including 61% of Republicans and 84% of Democrats — the highest and broadest public support of any single court reform option.

The implementation debate has two competing theories:

**The statutory theory** holds that Congress can impose 18-year limits by statute without a constitutional amendment, because justices retain their Article III commissions after moving to "senior status" on the lower courts — they continue to hold office during good behavior, they simply rotate out of the Supreme Court specifically. This theory was the dominant scholarly position before the recent conservative court shift; several constitutional law professors who previously held it have grown more skeptical given the current Court's expansive reading of executive power. The risk is that the Court would strike down a statutory term limits law as a usurpation of its own composition — and the current 6-3 supermajority would be deciding that question in its own interest.

**The constitutional amendment theory** avoids the legal uncertainty but requires a 2/3 vote of both chambers and ratification by 3/4 of states (38 states). Given that Democrats control fewer than 38 state governments at present, this path requires either substantial Republican support or a lengthy multi-year effort. The 73% public support figure suggests the political constituency exists; the legislative pathway requires sustained effort and likely waits for a period of greater political fluidity.

The strategic recommendation: pursue both tracks simultaneously. Pass the statutory version (H.R.1074) at the first opportunity of a Democratic trifecta, accepting the litigation risk because the policy itself is compelling and the legal theory is credible enough that five justices might uphold it. Simultaneously build the constitutional amendment campaign as a long-term project, using the state-level democratic innovation funds (Domain 9b) to build bipartisan coalitions at the state level. The constitutional amendment pathway becomes more viable if one or two Republican-appointed justices — facing the possibility that their own longevity on the court is being weighed against institutional legitimacy — break from the party line.

The 18-year staggered appointment model deserves specific design attention. Under the standard proposal: new vacancies occur in odd-numbered years (year 1 and year 3 of each presidential term), so every four-year president appoints exactly two justices. Justices serve 18 years and then rotate to senior status on the Courts of Appeals, where they can hear cases but cannot participate in Supreme Court decisions. If a president fills two vacancies per term, the composition of a 9-justice Court changes incrementally — no single appointment can determine outcomes for decades. The design also eliminates the grotesque incentive to appoint young ideologues with minimal judicial record: a 45-year-old appointed today would rotate off at 63, compared to a life appointment that might span 40+ years.

*Fiscal impact*: Court expansion costs (additional clerks, facilities, staff for senior status justices on Courts of Appeals) are negligible — under $50M/year system-wide. The public benefit of a court that evolves with legal thought and remains accountable to shifting democratic majorities is unquantifiable in fiscal terms but is the foundational precondition for every other domain in this proposal that depends on judicial enforcement. A Supreme Court that systematically strikes down democratic reforms (as the Loper Bright, CASA, immunity, and Shelby County decisions suggest it will) is not a neutral umpire — it is an obstacle to democratic renewal that must itself be reformed for the broader program to succeed.

**6i. Constitutional design principles for a more resilient judiciary: what Germany's Basic Law tells us.**

The Phase 3 comparative research identifies Germany's Basic Law as the most directly applicable design model for closing the specific structural gaps that have enabled the current judicial crisis. The Basic Law was written in 1949 with explicit knowledge of how the Weimar Republic's constitutional design failed — and every one of Weimar's failure modes has a US analog.

**The Weimar failure modes and their US analogs:**
- *Article 48 (emergency powers)* was invoked 205 times before Hitler became chancellor. No meaningful oversight existed; the president could govern by decree indefinitely. US analog: the National Emergencies Act with no enforcement mechanism; 37 active emergencies; $3.6B in military construction funds diverted for border wall construction; $49B in federal grants frozen by executive action.
- *Presidential removal authority*: Weimar's president could dismiss a chancellor who retained parliamentary confidence. US analog: removal of independent agency heads (Seila Law), Schedule F reclassification, the ongoing effort to establish plenary Article II removal authority.
- *The absence of a constructive vote of no confidence*: parliamentary majorities could bring down a government without forming a new one, enabling paralysis that extremists exploited. US analog: the separation of powers design that allows Congress to investigate and impeach but not to form an alternative government, creating a structural inability to respond to executive defiance.

**Basic Law's design responses — and their US equivalents:**
- *Emergency powers with judicial oversight*: The Federal Constitutional Court retains full authority during declared emergencies. No emergency can override the Court's jurisdiction. The Basic Law explicitly prohibits amendments that would abolish human dignity, the democratic order, or the federal structure (the "eternity clause," Article 79(3)). US equivalent needed: codification that federal courts retain jurisdiction during national emergencies, that emergency declarations cannot be used to redirect appropriated funds, and that specific constitutional provisions cannot be amended away by any process short of a new constitutional convention with full democratic legitimacy.
- *12-year non-renewable terms for Federal Constitutional Court judges*: 16 judges divided between two Senates, elected by two-thirds supermajorities in Bundestag and Bundesrat, serving non-renewable 12-year terms. The supermajority requirement forces inter-party consensus; the non-renewable terms eliminate post-appointment political incentives. US equivalent: the 18-year staggered term proposal in 6h, strengthened by a supermajority nomination mechanism analogous to Germany's — perhaps a bipartisan judicial appointments commission analogous to the UK's Judicial Appointments Commission that recommends candidates to the President, reducing pure partisan appointment.
- *Militant democracy (Streitbare Demokratie)*: Constitutional provisions allow banning of parties and organizations that seek to undermine the constitutional order. The Federal Constitutional Court may ban parties. US constitutional design currently has no equivalent — the First Amendment and ballot access doctrine make party banning essentially impossible, and the few available tools (insurrection clause, disqualification) have proven difficult to operationalize. The domestic equivalent is less a formal banning power than a strengthened framework for disqualification from federal office under Section 3 of the 14th Amendment, enforced through an independent process rather than through politically captured prosecutorial discretion.

**The specific design gap this reveals.** The US Constitution was written before the 20th century's demonstrations of how democracies die from within — before Weimar, before Hungary's Fidesz, before Turkey's AKP, before Poland's PiS. The constitutional design choices made in 1787 (life tenure, political appointment, no enforceable ethics code, no emergency power limits) were not obviously wrong in 1787. They are demonstrably wrong now. The Basic Law's designers had the advantage of knowing exactly how a constitutional democracy could be legally dismantled by a determined minority — and they designed against it. The US constitutional reform agenda should explicitly incorporate these 75 years of hard-won constitutional learning.

*Fiscal impact*: Constitutional design changes have no direct fiscal cost. Their indirect fiscal value — in institutional stability, rule-of-law credibility, and the economic growth differential that correlates with governance quality — is estimated at 0.5-1.0% annual GDP growth in cross-country studies (World Bank Governance Indicators). At US GDP scale, that is $125-250B/year in sustained growth premium.

*Source: `phase-3-research-roadmap.md`, Section 1B (Germany). [German Law Journal on the Basic Law](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/german-law-journal/article/basic-law-a-fifty-year-assessment/D4B8A18D8050DC8B453D13DCC66F5828); [Holocaust Encyclopedia on Article 48](https://encyclopedia.ushmm.org/content/en/article/article-48); [German Marshall Fund](https://www.gmfus.org/news/warning-basic-law).*

---

## Domain 7: Rights Protection and Civil Liberties

### The Problem

The current crisis has produced the most severe erosion of civil liberties in the United States since the Japanese internment during World War II. Executive overreach has targeted immigration enforcement, press freedom, political dissent, privacy, and the right to protest — not as isolated incidents but as a coordinated pattern of coercive state power directed at populations the administration views as political opponents.

**Immigration enforcement as civil liberties crisis.** The litigation tracker documents a systematic pattern of constitutional violations at industrial scale. ICE conducted an estimated 311,000 arrests in the first year of the current administration — a 400% increase over the prior year — with courts finding warrantless arrests without probable cause (CASA v. DHS, Ramirez Ovando v. Noem, ACLU of Ohio v. ICE), a government-adopted race-based arrest policy (Hussen v. Noem — the court confirmed that agents detained people "solely on the basis of their race" on at least 23 occasions), and forcible home entries authorized by an unpublished internal memo that agents were instructed not to memorialize in writing (Gibson v. ICE). Over 1,400 deportation flights have been conducted, including flights to third countries (El Salvador's CECOT prison) without deportation hearings — a practice that multiple federal courts have found violates due process. The detention population has exceeded 55,000, the highest in U.S. history, with documented conditions including 24-hour lighting, denial of legal counsel access, and medical neglect resulting in at least 14 deaths in custody in FY2025.

**Surveillance expansion.** The surveillance infrastructure has expanded dramatically: warrantless government purchases of commercial location data covering millions of Americans (documented in the Carpenter v. United States follow-on litigation), facial recognition deployment by CBP and ICE without congressional authorization, and the DOGE data integration project that merged databases across agencies — Social Security, IRS, DHS, Treasury — creating a comprehensive surveillance capability that no single agency was authorized to build. Section 702 of FISA was reauthorized in April 2024 without the warrant requirement for U.S. person queries that civil liberties organizations across the political spectrum demanded. An estimated 278,000 queries of U.S. persons' communications were conducted under Section 702 in FY2024.

**Criminalization of dissent and protest.** At least 21 states have enacted anti-protest laws since 2017, including felony penalties for pipeline protests, expanded definitions of "riot" that encompass peaceful assembly, and civil immunity for drivers who strike protesters blocking roads. At the federal level, the administration has invoked the Insurrection Act framework against protests on at least three occasions, deployed federal agents to cities over the objections of local officials, and classified domestic protest organizations as "domestic violent extremist" threats in intelligence bulletins — echoing the COINTELPRO-era practice of using national security classifications to surveil lawful political activity.

**Press freedom collapse.** The U.S. has been reclassified from "Low Restrictions" to "With Restrictions" by Reporters Without Borders (RSF), dropping to 55th globally — the lowest ranking in U.S. history. There were 170 assaults on journalists in 2025, with 160 by law enforcement. The DOJ has issued subpoenas for journalists' phone records, seized reporting materials from newsrooms, and revoked press credentials from outlets deemed insufficiently favorable. The White House has excluded specific news organizations from briefings — a selective access regime that transforms press coverage from a right into a privilege contingent on favorable coverage.

**The democratic consequence.** Rights protections are not ancillary to democracy — they are its precondition. Citizens who fear arrest for protest, surveillance for dissent, deportation for identity, or retaliation for reporting cannot exercise the political participation that Domains 1-3 envision. Every authoritarian consolidation in the 160-movement dataset began with the erosion of civil liberties before proceeding to institutional capture. The rights protection framework is the defensive perimeter that prevents backsliding after reform gains.

### Proposed Reforms

**7a. Constitutional floor for executive power.**

Codify (by statute, with constitutional amendment as the goal) explicit limits on executive emergency powers: mandatory congressional reauthorization of any emergency declaration after 30 days, prohibition on using emergency powers to redirect appropriated funds, prohibition on deploying military forces domestically without explicit congressional authorization, and automatic sunset of all emergency declarations after 180 days unless renewed by a two-thirds vote of both chambers.
