*Precedent*: France's état d'urgence framework requires parliamentary approval for any extension beyond 12 days and subjects all emergency measures to judicial review — the Conseil d'État reviews every administrative detention order. Germany's Basic Law (Article 20, the "resistance clause") explicitly authorizes citizens to resist attempts to abolish the constitutional order, and Article 80a restricts emergency economic powers to specific, enumerated circumstances. South Korea's constitution (Articles 76-77) requires National Assembly approval for emergency measures within 30 days and explicitly prohibits restricting press freedom or political party activity under emergency powers. Canada's Emergencies Act (2022 revision, after the trucker convoy invocation) requires parliamentary approval within 7 days, automatic judicial review, and mandatory public inquiry within 60 days of termination.

*Evidence*: The Brennan Center for Justice has documented 135 statutory emergency powers available to the U.S. president, many with no expiration date and no requirement for congressional approval. Thirty-seven national emergencies are currently active, some dating back decades. The National Emergencies Act of 1976 was intended to impose congressional oversight but included no enforcement mechanism — making it structurally unenforceable.

*Fiscal impact*: Negligible direct cost. The indirect fiscal impact of unconstrained emergency powers is substantial — the diversion of $3.6 billion in military construction funds for border wall construction (2019) and the freezing of $49 billion in federal grants (2025) demonstrate that emergency powers have become a mechanism for unilateral fiscal policy. Constraining this power protects congressional appropriations authority.

**7b. Immigration enforcement standards.**

Codify Fourth Amendment protections in immigration enforcement: warrants required for all arrests except at the border itself, probable cause determinations required for detention, right to counsel in all immigration proceedings, and mandatory judicial review within 48 hours of detention. Prohibit internal agency memos from establishing enforcement policies that bypass the Administrative Procedure Act's notice-and-comment requirements. Mandate body cameras for all immigration enforcement operations with retention requirements and civilian oversight review.

*Evidence*: The litigation tracker documents a systematic pattern. Courts have found warrantless arrests without probable cause (CASA v. DHS, Ramirez Ovando v. Noem, ACLU of Ohio v. ICE), a government-adopted race-based arrest policy (Hussen v. Noem — the court confirmed that agents detained people "solely on the basis of their race" on at least 23 occasions), and forcible home entries authorized by an unpublished internal memo that agents were instructed not to memorialize in writing (Gibson v. ICE, filed April 2026). The Gibson case is particularly significant: the May 2025 Lyons memo authorized home entries using Form I-205 (an administrative form signed by a DHS official, not a judge), was never published in the Federal Register, and was disseminated through verbal-only training — a structure designed to avoid both judicial review and discovery.

*Precedent*: The UK's Human Rights Act 1998 incorporates the European Convention on Human Rights into domestic law, requiring all immigration enforcement to comply with Article 5 (right to liberty) and Article 8 (right to private and family life). Canada's Immigration and Refugee Protection Act requires individualized risk assessments before detention, judicial review within 48 hours, and mandatory release unless the government demonstrates flight risk or danger — the burden is on the state, not the individual. New Zealand's immigration detention is subject to the Bill of Rights Act 1990, with mandatory habeas corpus review and strict time limits.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. The warrant requirement for home entries is already the constitutional standard — the reform codifies it so that future administrations cannot circumvent it through unpublished policy. The APA compliance requirement ensures that enforcement policies must survive public scrutiny.

*Fiscal impact*: Right to counsel in immigration proceedings costs an estimated $1.5-2.5B/year — but the Vera Institute's SAFE Network data shows that represented individuals are 5x more likely to be granted relief, dramatically reducing the cost of wrongful deportation (estimated at $10,000-50,000 per case in transportation, diplomatic, and legal costs when reversed). Body camera programs: $200-400M/year. Net savings from reduced litigation costs (the government lost 700+ cases in 2025-2026): substantial.

**7c. Privacy rights and anti-surveillance safeguards.**

Enact comprehensive federal privacy legislation: right to know what data the government holds about you, right to correct errors, right to see who accessed your data and why, prohibition on bulk surveillance without individualized warrants, mandatory data minimization for all government data collection, and a warrant requirement for all government purchases of commercially available personal data (closing the "data broker loophole" that circumvents the Fourth Amendment).

*Precedent*: The EU's GDPR provides a model for privacy regulation, with enforceable rights (access, correction, deletion, portability) and penalties up to 4% of global revenue. Estonia's digital identity system demonstrates that strong identity infrastructure can coexist with strong privacy protections — citizens can see in real time which government entities accessed their data and why, and unauthorized access is automatically flagged. Germany's Federal Constitutional Court has recognized an affirmative "right to informational self-determination" (Volkszählungsurteil, 1983) — a constitutional right to control one's own data that has no U.S. equivalent.

*Evidence*: The Georgetown Law Center on Privacy and Technology has documented that federal agencies conducted an estimated 3.8 million facial recognition searches in FY2024 without specific statutory authorization. The ACLU's investigation of CBP's use of commercial location data revealed that the agency purchased data covering the movements of millions of Americans — data that Carpenter v. United States (2018) established requires a warrant when obtained directly from carriers but that agencies circumvent by purchasing from data brokers.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal privacy enforcement infrastructure (modeled on EU data protection authorities): $500M-1B/year. The FTC's current privacy enforcement budget is approximately $50M — a fraction of what is needed. Revenue from privacy violation penalties (EU DPAs collected €4.5B in GDPR fines in 2023) would partially offset enforcement costs.

**7d. Whistleblower protection.**

Strengthen whistleblower protections for federal employees who document and expose illegal orders: legal defense funds, financial support during retaliation proceedings, accelerated judicial review of retaliation claims (within 90 days), burden-shifting (once a prima facie case of retaliation is established, the burden shifts to the agency to prove the action was not retaliatory), and protection for national security whistleblowers who currently fall outside the Whistleblower Protection Act.

*Precedent*: The EU Whistleblower Directive (2019/1937) requires all member states to establish protected reporting channels, prohibits retaliation, provides access to legal aid, and covers both public and private sector employees — including temporary workers and contractors. The UK's Employment Rights Act 1996 (Part IVA) provides "protected disclosure" status for whistleblowers, with uncapped compensation for retaliation and interim relief (continued employment pending adjudication). South Korea's Act on the Protection of Public Interest Whistleblowers provides financial rewards (up to $2M equivalent) for whistleblowers whose disclosures result in recovered funds, plus relocation assistance and identity protection.

*Evidence*: The Government Accountability Project reports that 83% of federal whistleblowers experience retaliation despite nominal protections. The Merit Systems Protection Board — the primary adjudicatory body for federal whistleblower claims — has lacked a quorum for most of the past decade, creating a backlog of over 3,500 cases. The DOGE-era mass firings included multiple documented cases of employees terminated within days of raising concerns about data access violations, waste, or illegal orders.

*Fiscal impact*: Expanded whistleblower protection infrastructure (MSPB staffing, legal defense fund, financial support): $200-400M/year. Return: the False Claims Act qui tam provisions — a form of whistleblower incentive — have recovered over $72 billion since 1986, making whistleblower protection one of the highest-ROI government investments.

**7e. Prohibition on weaponization of government data for partisan purposes.**

Establish by statute that government data collected for administrative purposes (tax records, benefits enrollment, immigration records, census data) cannot be accessed, shared, or used for electoral or partisan political purposes. Violations are a federal felony. Create an independent Data Protection Authority with audit power over all government data access, including access by temporary or embedded personnel (such as the DOGE model of embedding political operatives in agencies).

*Evidence*: The AFSCME v. SSA factual record is the clearest case. The DOJ acknowledged that DOGE associates improperly accessed Social Security Administration data, that a DOGE employee signed an agreement to share SSA data with "an unnamed political advocacy group that wanted to overturn election results in certain states," that access occurred via an unauthorized server outside official protocols, and that SSA itself still does not know the full scope of what DOGE accessed or shared. The DOJ further conceded that defendants had provided "patently false information to the district court" during injunction proceedings. This is not a hypothetical risk — it is a documented instance of federal administrative data being channeled to partisan electoral operations, with active judicial deception to conceal it.

*Precedent*: Germany's Federal Commissioner for Data Protection and Freedom of Information (BfDI) operates as an independent authority with the power to audit all government data processing, issue binding orders, and impose fines — including against intelligence agencies. The UK's Information Commissioner's Office (ICO) investigated the Cambridge Analytica scandal and demonstrated the capacity to hold both private companies and political actors accountable for data misuse. Brazil's Lei Geral de Proteção de Dados (LGPD, 2020) created the Autoridade Nacional de Proteção de Dados (ANPD) with specific jurisdiction over government data processing, including electoral data.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. The political case is strong: government data weaponization is unpopular across the political spectrum. The technical implementation requires audit logging (who accessed what data, when, and why) and an independent authority with real-time monitoring capability — architecture that also serves the open government and transparency goals in Domain 4.

*Fiscal impact*: Independent Data Protection Authority: $300-500M/year (comparable to the EU model, adjusted for scale). Revenue from enforcement penalties and deterrence of fraud enabled by data misuse would offset. The DOGE data breach alone — if the compromised Social Security data is used for identity theft — could cost affected individuals and the government billions in remediation.

**7f. Right to protest and peaceful assembly.**

Establish by federal statute an affirmative right to protest and peaceful assembly on public property, preempting state laws that criminalize protected First Amendment activity. Specifically: prohibit felony penalties for peaceful protest, prohibit civil immunity for violence against protesters, prohibit "critical infrastructure" trespass laws that define public roads and sidewalks as protected infrastructure during protests, require law enforcement to facilitate (not suppress) peaceful assembly, prohibit kettling and mass arrest tactics that do not distinguish between lawful and unlawful conduct, and mandate de-escalation protocols for all law enforcement interactions with protesters.

*Evidence*: The International Center for Not-for-Profit Law (ICNL) has documented that 45 U.S. states have considered anti-protest bills since 2017, with 21 enacting laws that restrict protest rights beyond existing criminal law. These include: felony penalties for pipeline protests (Louisiana, Oklahoma, Texas, West Virginia), expanded "riot" definitions that encompass any gathering where property damage occurs — regardless of individual conduct (Florida, Iowa, Tennessee), and civil immunity for drivers who strike protesters blocking roads (Florida, Iowa, Oklahoma). The ACLU's analysis of the 2020 protest response documented over 900 instances of police violence against protesters and journalists across 400+ incidents in a single month — the largest mass civil liberties violation in modern U.S. history.

*Precedent*: Germany's Basic Law (Article 8) guarantees the right of assembly without registration or permission for indoor gatherings, with outdoor assemblies requiring only notification (not permission). The Federal Constitutional Court has repeatedly ruled that the state has an obligation to facilitate, not merely tolerate, peaceful assembly — including an obligation to protect protesters from counter-protesters. South Korea's Constitutional Court struck down blanket bans on nighttime protests in 2014 (2009 Hun-Ga 17), finding them a disproportionate restriction on assembly rights. The European Court of Human Rights has established that states have a positive obligation under Article 11 to protect peaceful assembly, and that the possibility of violent counter-demonstrations cannot justify banning a peaceful protest (Bączkowski v. Poland, 2007).

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute (Right to Peaceful Assembly Act). First Amendment protections provide the constitutional foundation, but the patchwork of state anti-protest laws has created a situation where the practical ability to protest varies enormously by jurisdiction. Federal preemption of the most egregious restrictions — combined with affirmative facilitation requirements for law enforcement — would restore a uniform national standard.

*Fiscal impact*: Negligible direct cost — the reform primarily constrains state action rather than creating new federal programs. Law enforcement de-escalation training: $50-100M/year through DOJ grants. The fiscal benefit of protecting protest rights is difficult to quantify but historically enormous — every major reform in U.S. history, from abolition to women's suffrage to the Civil Rights Act to marriage equality, was achieved through sustained protest. The cost of suppressing protest is paid in democratic legitimacy.

**7g. Campaign finance structural overhaul: from oligarchic to democratic funding.**

The current campaign finance system is not merely an imperfection in an otherwise functional democracy. It is the mechanism by which concentrated wealth converts into legislative protection of concentrated wealth — a feedback loop that makes every other reform in this document harder to achieve. Martin Gilens and Benjamin Page's 2014 finding is the precise empirical description of its effect: after controlling for elite preferences, median voter preferences have near-zero predictive power over policy outcomes. This is not a coincidence of preferences. It is the result of a funding architecture that makes legislators structurally accountable to donors between elections and to voters only during the brief window of campaign season.

The scale of the problem as of the 2024 election cycle: total federal election spending reached approximately $15.9 billion (OpenSecrets), with dark money — contributions from nonprofits and trade associations that do not disclose their donors — hitting a record high of $1.9 billion (Brennan Center for Justice, "Dark Money Hit a Record High of $1.9 Billion in 2024 Federal Races"). This nearly doubled the $1 billion in dark money spent in 2020. Super PACs spent a record $2.7 billion, with $1.3 billion flowing to super PACs from shell companies and nonprofits that disclosed no underlying donors — more than in the prior two election cycles combined. Since Citizens United v. FEC (2010), dark money groups have spent at least $4.3 billion on federal elections. One hundred billionaire families were responsible for approximately one in six dollars spent in the 2022 cycle (OpenSecrets analysis); the concentration in 2024 was at least as severe.

The legal chain that produced this system requires understanding as a precondition for reform. Buckley v. Valeo (1976) established that spending money in elections is a form of political speech protected by the First Amendment, and that limits on independent spending — spending not coordinated with candidates — are unconstitutional. First National Bank of Boston v. Bellotti (1978) extended First Amendment political speech rights to corporations. Citizens United v. FEC (2010) held that corporations and unions cannot be prohibited from making independent expenditures in elections. McCutcheon v. FEC (2014) struck down aggregate limits on individual contributions to multiple candidates and party committees. Each decision narrowed the constitutional space for campaign finance regulation; together they created a system where unlimited money flows into elections through disclosed (super PACs), semi-disclosed (party committees), and fully undisclosed (501(c)(4) nonprofits and trade associations) channels.

The current system cannot be fully reformed without a constitutional amendment — but substantial reform is available through statute within existing doctrine, and the statutory reforms are the necessary precondition for the longer-term constitutional project.

**Immediate statutory reforms — within current doctrine**:

*Full disclosure*: The DISCLOSE Act of 2026, reintroduced by Senator Sheldon Whitehouse and Representative Chris Pappas with all 47 Democratic senators and 139 House members as cosponsors, would require organizations making expenditures of more than $10,000 in an election cycle to report within 24 hours, disclose the identity of donors who gave more than $10,000, and extend disclosure requirements to paid digital political communications including social media influencer payments. The 2026 version closes the specific loophole used by 501(c)(4) "social welfare" organizations to run electoral advertising without disclosure: it applies to any organization whose expenditures are "electioneering communications" regardless of stated organizational purpose. The political case for disclosure is strong across party lines: 78% of Americans support requiring dark money donors to disclose their identities (Gallup, 2023). The Senate's filibuster has blocked the DISCLOSE Act in every Congress since 2010 — making filibuster reform (Domain 6) and disclosure reform mutually reinforcing.

*Small-dollar public financing*: The Government By the People Act (introduced in the 119th Congress) would establish a federal small-dollar public financing system with a 6:1 federal match for contributions of $200 or less. This directly addresses the structural problem: candidates who raise 70% of their funds from small donors behave differently than candidates who raise 70% of their funds from bundled max-out donations of $3,300. New York City's 8:1 small-dollar matching system, operational since 1988, has produced the most diverse candidate pool of any major city in the country — because candidates who can win by organizing their communities do not need to be acceptable to major donors before entering the race. A 2019 evaluation by the Citizens Union found that NYC matching funds reduced the influence of lobbyists, PACs, and real estate interests in city council races by approximately 40% compared to council races in comparable cities without matching. Connecticut's Citizens' Election Program, established in 2005, provides full public financing for candidates who raise a threshold number of small contributions. Participation has exceeded 80% of legislative candidates in competitive races; candidate diversity increased significantly in the first cycle. Maine's Clean Election Act (1996) operates similarly — over 80% of state legislators have used the program, and Maine consistently ranks among the top 10 states for public confidence in government (Gallup State of the States).

*Shareholder democracy and corporate disclosure*: Require all publicly traded corporations to disclose political expenditures to shareholders and obtain shareholder approval for political spending above $50,000. Currently, executives can make political contributions from corporate treasuries without shareholder knowledge or consent. The Securities and Exchange Commission received a petition for a rulemaking on corporate political disclosure in 2011 signed by over 500,000 individuals — the largest petition in SEC history — and declined to act after industry lobbying. The statutory approach: amend the Securities Exchange Act of 1934 to require disclosure of political spending as material information.

*FEC structural reform*: The Federal Election Commission, the body responsible for enforcing campaign finance law, is structurally designed to be deadlocked. Its six commissioners are appointed in equal partisan numbers, requiring a bipartisan majority for enforcement actions — which means the party whose donors are being investigated can block enforcement by maintaining partisan cohesion. Between 2009 and 2023, FEC Republican commissioners voted as a bloc to block enforcement in over 90% of contested cases (Brennan Center analysis). The reform: restructure the FEC as a five-member independent body with staggered, non-renewable 7-year terms, appointed by a bipartisan commission of retired federal judges rather than by the president with Senate confirmation along partisan lines. The five-member structure (with no partisan tie) eliminates the deadlock by design.

**Long-term structural reforms — requiring constitutional amendment**:

A constitutional amendment overturning Citizens United must address two distinct holdings: (1) that corporations have First Amendment rights equivalent to natural persons in the political context, and (2) that spending money is political speech that cannot be regulated as conduct. The amendment does not need to eliminate free speech protection for political spending — it can instead restore Congress's authority to regulate "the time, place, and manner" of campaign finance as it regulates other forms of conduct that affect democracy. Twenty-two states and over 800 municipalities have passed resolutions supporting such an amendment. The movement for a constitutional amendment requires a two-thirds vote of both chambers or a constitutional convention called by two-thirds of states, followed by ratification by three-quarters of states — a multi-decade project that requires sustained organizing. The statutory reforms above are both independently valuable and create the political conditions for the constitutional campaign.

*International benchmarks*: Canada prohibits corporate and union contributions to political parties entirely (Canada Elections Act, 2003 amendment) and caps individual contributions at C$1,725/year per party (indexed to inflation). The result: Canadian federal elections cost approximately $0.50-$1 per eligible voter to run, compared to $45 per eligible voter in the 2024 U.S. federal elections — a 45-90x cost differential that reflects the absence of unlimited outside spending. Germany prohibits all corporate donations to political parties (Parteiengesetz), limits individual donations to €100,000/year per party, requires public disclosure of all donations above €10,000, and provides state party funding based on vote share and small-donor matching — ensuring parties are financially accountable to voters rather than donors. France prohibits corporate donations to political campaigns entirely and caps individual donations at €4,600/election per candidate plus €7,500/year to parties, with public financing covering 50-60% of major party operating costs. The UK's Political Parties, Elections and Referendums Act (PPERA, 2000) limits total campaign spending to approximately £30 million nationally per party per general election, with detailed disclosure requirements enforced by an independent Electoral Commission.

*Fiscal impact*: Small-dollar public financing system (6:1 federal match for contributions under $200): estimated $3-5B/election cycle, or $1.5-2.5B/year amortized. FEC restructuring: negligible — current FEC budget is approximately $100M/year; restructuring adds governance reform, not budget. Disclosure system (DISCLOSE Act compliance infrastructure): $50-100M/year at the FEC. The return is not fiscal but democratic: campaigns funded by small donors produce legislators who are structurally accountable to constituents rather than donors. The Gilens-Page finding implies that policy outcomes are currently determined by the top 5-10% of income earners' preferences. Even partial correction of this structural bias — through small-dollar matching — generates incalculable democratic value.

**7h. Congressional ethics enforcement: replacing theater with accountability.**

The congressional ethics system has operated for two decades as a mechanism for absorbing scandal and producing no consequences. The evidence is unambiguous: the Senate Ethics Committee did not issue a single disciplinary sanction in 2025, maintaining a streak of nearly two decades without meaningful punishment despite numerous documented violations (NOTUS investigation, 2025). Of 181 alleged Senate rules violations reviewed by the committee in recent years, it issued no letters of admonition and dismissed 150 cases for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The House's Office of Congressional Conduct (formerly the Office of Congressional Ethics, renamed in the 119th Congress) found evidence of violations in 43% of cases it reviewed — compared to the Senate Ethics Committee's 3% finding rate — illustrating the difference between an outside body applying facts to the law and a self-policing body with structural incentives to find nothing.

The STOCK Act, enacted in 2012 to prohibit congressional insider trading, has produced no prosecutions in thirteen years. Its $200 penalty for late filing is the enforcement mechanism for what is potentially millions of dollars in trading on non-public information. The evidence of the problem is direct and recent: at least 25 members of Congress made stock trades between President Trump's April 2025 tariff announcement and the subsequent pause — trades that yielded significant profits as markets surged on the pause news. Attorney General Pam Bondi sold Trump Media stock just before the tariff announcement. Marjorie Taylor Greene bought stocks during the post-announcement market dip that subsequently surged after the pause. The House Administration Committee held a hearing in November 2025 — the first substantive hearing on congressional stock trading since the STOCK Act's passage — but no legislation passed before the year's end.

The structural problem is the same as for any self-policing system: the people who must pass the rules governing their conduct are the people the rules govern. Congressional ethics reform requires institutional design that removes legislators from the enforcement of rules against themselves.

**The independent ethics commission model**: Replace the House Ethics Committee (which is composed of members of Congress judging their colleagues) and the Senate Ethics Committee (which is the most captured self-policing body in American government) with an independent congressional ethics commission. The commission would have:

- Seven members: two appointed by the Speaker, two by the Minority Leader (House), two by equivalent Senate leadership, and one independent chair appointed by a bipartisan panel of retired federal judges — with no member having held elected or appointed federal office within the prior 10 years.
- Independent investigative staff with subpoena authority.
- Authority to refer violations for criminal prosecution to the DOJ Public Integrity Section (which must respond within 180 days with a prosecution decision or a written declination with reasons).
- Authority to impose sanctions directly, including censure, fines, and recommendation of expulsion (which still requires a two-thirds vote of the relevant chamber under Article I, Section 5 — this cannot be changed by statute).
- Protected funding — budget set by statute at 0.5% of the combined congressional operating budget, not subject to riders or continuing resolutions.

The model is the UK's Parliamentary Commissioner for Standards, an independent officer who investigates complaints against Members of Parliament, reports findings to the Standards Committee, and whose reports are publicly released even when the Committee disagrees with the findings. The Australian National Anti-Corruption Commission (NACC, established 2023) provides an even stronger model: it has jurisdiction over all public officials including members of Parliament, independent investigative authority, public hearing power, and prosecution referral authority. The U.S. equivalent should extend the commission's jurisdiction to cover all legislative branch personnel — members, staff, and lobbyists operating in the congressional ecosystem.

**STOCK Act replacement — the congressional trading ban**: The most effective solution to congressional stock trading is not better disclosure but prohibition. At least 25 bills have been introduced in the 119th Congress to ban or limit member stock trading. The strongest version — the No Getting Rich in Congress Act — would prohibit members of Congress and their spouses from owning individual securities, requiring divestiture into index funds or blind trusts within 180 days of taking office. This is the structure that the executive branch already applies to Cabinet secretaries and senior White House officials. The argument against extension to Congress is that members are responsible for their own financial management — but this ignores the fundamental conflict of interest between legislative decisions affecting specific companies and personal ownership of those companies' securities. Poll data on the congressional trading ban shows consistent bipartisan public support: 67-76% of Americans across partisan lines support prohibiting member stock trading (various polls, 2022-2025).

The narrower but politically more achievable version: prohibit trading in any sector that a member serves on a committee with jurisdiction over. Armed Services Committee members would be prohibited from trading defense stocks; Finance Committee members from trading in financial stocks; Energy and Commerce members from trading in energy and pharmaceutical stocks. This is not a perfect solution — members can shift to stock picking in non-committee sectors — but it directly addresses the most documented form of insider trading by legislators.

**Revolving door reform**: The 1-year lobbying ban on former members of Congress (2 years for Senators) is the weakest revolving door restriction among peer democracies. Germany requires former federal ministers to wait 18 months (with the government having authority to extend to 3 years in cases where conflicts are substantial) before accepting positions in private industry. France's HATVP reviews and publicly discloses all post-government employment for senior officials within 2 months of departure. The EU requires Commissioners to observe a 2-year ban on lobbying on matters they handled and a lifetime ban on lobbying their own institution on their specific file. The U.S. standard should be extended to: a 5-year ban on lobbying Congress for former members and 2-year ban for senior staff; a 3-year ban on employment at any company the former official directly regulated or awarded contracts to; prohibition on family members of sitting members from being registered lobbyists; and a public registry of all lobbying contacts between lobbyists and the members or staff they previously worked for.

**Financial disclosure reform**: Current financial disclosure filings are annual, paper-based, and frequently filed late — the $200 penalty for late filing is a trivial deterrent. Reform should require: quarterly electronic disclosure of all financial transactions above $1,000; automated flagging by an independent compliance office when transactions involve companies in sectors under committee jurisdiction; public-facing searchable database (the current system requires manual searching of PDFs); and penalties escalating to $50,000 for late filings and criminal referral for intentional misreporting.

*Precedent*: New Zealand's parliament requires members to disclose all financial interests within 30 days of election, update annually with any changes, and the register is publicly searchable online with category-level specificity (not just "stocks" but specific companies). Singapore's President and ministers are required to disclose all assets and have them valued independently. Canada's Conflict of Interest and Ethics Commissioner is an independent parliamentary officer who actively investigates conflicts, publishes findings, and has the power to recommend salary forfeiture for violations.

*Fiscal impact*: Independent Congressional Ethics Commission: $100-150M/year (comparable to the UK Parliamentary Commissioner for Standards system at scale for the U.S. legislative branch). Enhanced disclosure system: $20-30M/year in IT infrastructure. Stock trading ban enforcement infrastructure: $10-20M/year. Total: $130-200M/year. The return is not primarily fiscal but democratic: a Congress whose members are prohibited from trading on the information they receive while making legislation is a Congress that has reduced one of the most direct mechanisms by which financial interests capture legislative outcomes. The Ziobrowski pre-STOCK Act study found that Congressional stock portfolios outperformed the market by 6% annually (Senators) and 4% annually (Representatives) — demonstrating that the information advantage was real, and that STOCK Act disclosure without trading prohibition has not eliminated it.

---

## Domain 8: Media and Information Ecosystem

*Evidence companion: `domain-deepening/domain-08-media-information.md` — full evidence library for the media and information ecosystem crisis and reform tools. Covers the Brookings/Notre Dame municipal borrowing cost study in full methodological detail (Gao, Lee, and Murphy; 5-11 basis point finding; research design; $650M/year derivation); Medill 2024 news desert data (208 zero-news counties, 55M Americans with limited access, 127 closures in 2024); Alden Global Capital portfolio and business model (Tribune acquisition, margin targets, UNC staff-cut data); González-Bailón et al. 2023 Science study on Facebook algorithm asymmetric ideological segregation (208M users; asymmetry finding — conservative exclusive corner vs. no liberal equivalent; misinformation concentration); Frances Haugen disclosure anatomy (teen mental health internal data, 2020 election safeguard rollbacks, what the company knew and chose); RSF reclassification history (17th in 2002 to 57th in 2025; "problematic situation" classification; immigration-journalism intersection); Moody v. NetChoice (2024) — what SCOTUS actually held about platform editorial discretion and what it does/does not foreclose for regulation; Nordic press subsidy formulas in granular detail (Sweden's Presstödsnämnden eligibility formula, 40% cost cap, political exclusion rule); ARD/ZDF Rundfunkbeitrag and the 2018 Federal Constitutional Court ruling (1 BvR 1675/16); EU Digital Services Act enforcement record including first €120M fine against X (December 2025); Finland media literacy curriculum grade-by-grade; Estonia's dual-track digital resilience response to 2007 cyberattacks and Russian information operations; four-element board insulation architecture and why all four are required; shield law "imminent physical harm" standard in practice; and media literacy curriculum at grade-level specificity.*

### The Problem

The United States faces an information ecosystem crisis that threatens the foundational capacity for democratic self-governance. This crisis operates on three fronts simultaneously — the destruction of local news infrastructure, the dominance of algorithmically curated outrage content, and the active suppression of press freedom by the state — and each front reinforces the others.

**Local news collapse.** Over 2,900 newspapers have closed since 2005, leaving approximately 1,800 communities as "news deserts" with no local newspaper coverage of government, courts, schools, or police. U.S. newsroom employment has declined from 71,640 in 2008 to approximately 31,000 in 2025 — a 57% reduction. The economic model that sustained local journalism (classified advertising, local retail advertising) has been captured by digital platforms that extract the revenue without producing the journalism. The consequences are measurable: a Brookings Institution study found that municipal borrowing costs increase 5-11 basis points in communities that lose newspaper coverage (an estimated $650 million in additional interest costs annually). University of Chicago and University of Illinois researchers documented a 4% increase in government waste in communities that lost local newspapers. Voter turnout declines 3-10% in areas without local news coverage. Local news deserts correlate with reduced civic engagement, lower government accountability, and increased political polarization — residents in news deserts are more reliant on national partisan media for information about their own communities.

**Algorithmic amplification of division.** Approximately 62% of U.S. adults now get news from social media platforms, where content is curated by engagement-optimization algorithms that systematically amplify outrage, fear, and division over accuracy and context. Internal Facebook documents (the 2021 Frances Haugen disclosures) confirmed that the company's own research showed its algorithms amplified divisive content, that the company was aware of the harm, and that it chose engagement metrics over user welfare. A 2023 Science study (González-Bailón et al.) found that Facebook's recommendation algorithm increased exposure to ideologically concordant content by 15-20% and reduced exposure to cross-cutting content. The concentration of media distribution in a handful of platforms — Google and Meta control approximately 50% of all digital advertising revenue, with Google's search algorithm determining what 90% of Americans see first — means that editorial decisions made by a small number of companies shape the information diet of the entire nation.

**State suppression of press freedom.** The U.S. has been reclassified from "Low Restrictions" to "With Restrictions" by Reporters Without Borders (RSF), dropping to 55th globally. There were 170 assaults on journalists in 2025, with 160 by law enforcement. The DOJ has issued subpoenas for journalists' phone records, seized reporting materials, and revoked press credentials from outlets deemed insufficiently favorable. The Committee to Protect Journalists (CPJ) documented 43 journalists arrested or detained during the 2025 protest coverage period — the highest number in a single year in U.S. history.

**Media consolidation.** Six companies control approximately 90% of the media Americans consume. Private equity acquisition of local media has accelerated closures and gutted newsrooms — hedge fund-owned newspaper chains (Alden Global Capital, the largest newspaper owner in the U.S.) have cut staffing by 50-70% at acquired papers while extracting profit margins of 15-25%. When Elon Musk acquired Twitter/X and changed its algorithmic priorities, he single-handedly altered the information diet of 350 million users — a demonstration of concentrated media power incompatible with democratic self-governance.

**The democratic consequence.** The research on democratic movements consistently identifies information control as a precondition for authoritarian consolidation: every successful authoritarian project in the 160-movement dataset either captured or destroyed independent media before completing institutional takeover. Hungary's Fidesz built its media dominance before its democratic majority became unassailable. Turkey's AKP systematically pressured and acquired independent media outlets before the 2017 constitutional referendum that consolidated presidential power. The U.S. pattern is different in form — market-driven collapse rather than state acquisition — but the functional result is converging: a population without the shared factual foundation required for democratic self-governance.

### Proposed Reforms

**8a. Public interest media funding.**

Establish a federal public interest media endowment (modeled on the BBC's license fee or the Australian Broadcasting Corporation's government funding) that provides sustained, independent funding for nonprofit local journalism, investigative reporting, and civic information infrastructure. Annual funding of $5-10 billion (approximately $15-30 per citizen) would transform the information landscape. Funding should be allocated through an independent board with fixed terms, bipartisan appointment, and a statutory prohibition on editorial interference — modeled on the insulation mechanisms recommended for independent agencies in Domain 2.

*Precedent*: The BBC operates on approximately $6 billion annually (funded by license fee, not government appropriation) and is consistently ranked among the world's most trusted news sources. ARD/ZDF (Germany) receives approximately €8 billion/year through a constitutionally protected per-household fee, funding the largest public broadcasting system in the world — covering television, radio, digital, and regional programming. NHK (Japan) operates on a mandatory household subscription fee (~$130/year) that is set by the Diet but collected independently. The Nordic model provides direct subsidies to private news organizations through independent boards: Sweden's Press Subsidies Board (Presstödsnämnden, operating since 1971) distributes approximately $80M/year; Norway's Media Authority distributes approximately $45M/year; Denmark's Media Agreement provides approximately $70M/year. All three countries rank in the top 5 globally for press freedom and democratic trust. Report for America and the Local Independent Online News (LION) publishers demonstrate that nonprofit local journalism can be effective and sustainable with adequate funding.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute creating an independent endowment with a board insulated from political interference (fixed terms, bipartisan appointment, protected funding). The endowment model (rather than annual appropriation) is critical — it must be structured so that a hostile future administration cannot defund independent media the way it defunded USAID.

*Fiscal impact*: $5-7B/year at BBC-equivalent per-capita spending ($15-20 per citizen). Current U.S. public media funding (CPB: $535M/year) is the lowest per-capita public media investment in the OECD — less than $2 per citizen compared to the UK's $70, Germany's $95, and the Nordic average of $60-80. The cost of the information vacuum is already being paid: $650M/year in higher municipal borrowing costs (Brookings), billions in undetected government waste (University of Chicago), and incalculable democratic costs from uninformed electorates.

**8b. Platform accountability.**

Require algorithmic transparency from platforms above 50 million U.S. users: mandatory disclosure of recommendation algorithms, independent auditing of algorithmic amplification effects, prohibition on algorithmic targeting of political content to minors, and mandatory "chronological feed" option for all users. Require platforms to provide researcher access to data on content distribution and amplification patterns, with appropriate privacy protections. Establish a Digital Platform Accountability Office within the FTC with dedicated staffing and expertise.

*Evidence*: Internal Facebook documents (the 2021 Frances Haugen disclosures) confirmed that the company's own research showed its algorithms amplified divisive and outrage-inducing content, that it was aware of the harm, and that it chose engagement metrics over user welfare. Algorithmic amplification is not neutral curation — it is an editorial decision made at scale, and it should be subject to accountability proportional to its influence. A 2024 NYU study found that platform recommender systems increase user exposure to misinformation by 70% compared to chronological feeds.

*Precedent*: The EU's Digital Services Act (DSA, 2022) provides a working model: it requires very large platforms (VLOPs, those with 45M+ EU users) to conduct systemic risk assessments, submit to independent audits, and provide researcher access to data. The European Commission designated 19 VLOPs in 2023 and has already opened formal proceedings against X/Twitter and TikTok. Australia's Online Safety Act (2021) created an eSafety Commissioner with investigative and enforcement powers. South Korea's Telecommunications Business Act requires platforms to disclose recommendation algorithm criteria and provide opt-out mechanisms.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. The U.S. can adopt a DSA-equivalent framework adapted to First Amendment requirements — focusing on transparency and accountability for algorithmic systems rather than content regulation.

*Fiscal impact*: Digital Platform Accountability Office: $200-400M/year (comparable to the EU's DSA enforcement infrastructure). Revenue from platform accountability fines (the EU has assessed potential penalties in the billions under the DSA) would offset. The societal cost of unregulated algorithmic amplification — measured in political polarization, mental health impacts ($11B/year estimated cost for adolescent mental health effects alone, per the Surgeon General's 2023 advisory), and democratic erosion — dwarfs the regulatory investment.

**8c. Civic information infrastructure.**

Fund the development of community information networks — local group chats, email lists, community radio, digital town squares — that provide reliable local information when national media is compromised. This is information infrastructure, not content regulation. The goal is resilient local information flows that survive the failure of any single platform or media outlet. Establish a Community Information Fund ($500M/year) for grants to local civic technology projects, community radio stations, and neighborhood information networks.

*Evidence*: Taiwan's g0v civic tech community and the vTaiwan deliberation platform demonstrate that civic information infrastructure can be built as public goods. During the COVID-19 pandemic, Taiwan's civic tech community built mask availability maps, real-time information dashboards, and fact-checking infrastructure faster than the government could have — because the infrastructure was designed to enable citizen participation rather than centralized broadcast. Barcelona's Decidim platform (400+ organizations, 150,000+ participants) demonstrates that open-source civic technology can operate at metropolitan scale. In the U.S., community radio (1,500+ LPFM stations) and the emerging network of local nonprofit newsrooms provide a foundation to build on. The Knight Foundation's research shows that communities with strong local information ecosystems have 25-40% higher civic engagement rates.

*Precedent*: South Korea's Community Media Foundation provides government funding for community broadcasting and citizen journalism training, operating 18 community media centers across the country. The UK's Community Radio Fund (Ofcom-administered) has supported 300+ community stations since 2004. Canada's Community Access Program provided public internet access and digital literacy training to underserved communities before its 2012 defunding — a cautionary example of what happens when civic infrastructure lacks protected funding.

*Fiscal impact*: Community Information Fund: $500M/year. Community radio infrastructure: $100M/year. Digital civic infrastructure (open-source platforms, hosting, maintenance): $50-100M/year. Total: $650-700M/year — approximately $2 per citizen annually. The return is measured in civic engagement, government accountability, and community resilience rather than direct fiscal savings.

**8d. Press freedom protections.**

Establish by statute a federal shield law protecting journalists from compelled disclosure of sources, with narrow exceptions only for imminent physical harm. Criminalize the use of federal resources to surveil, intimidate, or retaliate against journalists. Create an independent press freedom ombudsman with authority to investigate complaints, publish findings, and refer cases for prosecution. Mandate that all federal law enforcement agencies adopt specific protocols for interactions with journalists at protests and public events, including a prohibition on targeting press equipment and a requirement for designated press liaisons at major events.

*Evidence*: The RSF reclassification and the 170 documented assaults on journalists in 2025 (predominantly by law enforcement) indicate that informal norms protecting press freedom have collapsed. Statutory protection is required because the First Amendment, while providing a constitutional floor, has not prevented the pattern of intimidation and violence documented in the crisis analysis. Only 34 states and the District of Columbia have shield laws; the absence of a federal shield law means that federal prosecutors can compel journalists to reveal sources in federal investigations — a power that has been used to chill national security reporting.

*Precedent*: Sweden's Freedom of the Press Act (Tryckfrihetsförordningen, 1766) is the world's oldest press freedom law and is part of Sweden's constitution — making press freedom a fundamental law that cannot be altered by ordinary legislation. It includes absolute source protection: it is a criminal offense for any government official to attempt to identify a journalist's source, and journalists face imprisonment if they voluntarily disclose sources. Norway's constitution (Section 100) provides explicit press freedom protections, and the Norwegian Media Authority operates independently of the government. The European Court of Human Rights has consistently held that source protection is a fundamental condition for press freedom (Goodwin v. United Kingdom, 1996; Voskuil v. Netherlands, 2007).

*Fiscal impact*: Press freedom ombudsman office: $20-50M/year. Law enforcement press interaction training: $30-50M/year through DOJ grants. The fiscal return of press freedom — measured in corruption deterred, government accountability maintained, and informed democratic participation enabled — is incalculable. The empirical evidence cited in 8a (higher borrowing costs, increased waste, lower turnout) demonstrates the measurable costs when press freedom erodes.

**8e. Media literacy and critical thinking education.**

Mandate and fund media literacy education in all federally funded schools, starting in elementary school and continuing through secondary education. Curriculum should cover: identifying reliable sources, understanding algorithmic curation, recognizing disinformation techniques, distinguishing news from opinion, understanding media ownership and business models, and developing habits of verification before sharing. Fund teacher training, curriculum development, and ongoing assessment of media literacy outcomes. Connect to the civic education reforms in Domain 10.

*Evidence*: Stanford University's evaluations of student media literacy (2016, 2019, 2022) found that a majority of U.S. students at every level — from middle school through college — could not reliably distinguish news articles from advertisements, identify the source of a claim, or evaluate the credibility of a website. The National Association for Media Literacy Education reports that only 14 states include media literacy in their educational standards, and most of those do so superficially. Meanwhile, the proliferation of AI-generated content, deepfakes, and synthetic media is accelerating — making media literacy not a supplementary skill but a survival capability for democratic citizenship.

*Precedent*: Finland's media literacy education program is the global gold standard. Media literacy has been integrated into the national curriculum since the 1970s and was made a mandatory cross-curricular theme in 2016. Finnish students learn to analyze media from first grade through high school — covering source evaluation, understanding bias, recognizing propaganda techniques, and creating their own media content. Finland consistently ranks #1 on the Open Society Institute's Media Literacy Index and #1-3 on the World Press Freedom Index. The correlation is not coincidental: citizens who can evaluate information critically are both less susceptible to manipulation and more capable of holding media accountable. Estonia's media literacy program, developed after Russian disinformation campaigns targeting the Russian-speaking minority, has become a model for digital resilience — integrating cybersecurity, source verification, and critical thinking into a unified curriculum. Canada's MediaSmarts (formerly the Media Awareness Network), a government-funded nonprofit, has developed K-12 media literacy resources used in all provinces and territories.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute conditioning education funding (ESEA/ESSA) on adoption of evidence-based media literacy standards, with federal grants for curriculum development, teacher training, and assessment tools. The Department of Education (or its successor, if abolished and reformed per Domain 10) would develop model curricula in partnership with media literacy organizations and academic researchers.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal grants for curriculum development and teacher training: $200-400M/year. Ongoing assessment and program evaluation: $50M/year. Total: $250-450M/year — less than $1.50 per citizen annually. The return is measured in democratic resilience: Finland's investment in media literacy has made it the most resistant country in Europe to disinformation campaigns, despite sharing a 1,340-kilometer border with Russia and being a primary target of Russian information operations.

---

## Domain 9: Federalism and Local Democracy

### The Problem

American federalism is simultaneously a strength (distributed power, policy experimentation, local responsiveness) and a vulnerability (states can suppress voting rights, restrict civil liberties, and entrench minority rule). The current crisis has exposed both dimensions with unusual clarity — and revealed a third problem: the systematic destruction of local democratic authority by state governments that claim to champion local control.

**Federalism as rights suppression.** Since Shelby County v. Holder (2013) weakened federal voting rights oversight, states have used their authority over elections to restrict voting access with surgical precision. The Brennan Center documented 94 restrictive voting laws enacted in 29 states between 2013 and 2024. Texas closed 750 polling places, disproportionately in communities of color. Georgia's SB 202 (2021) restricted ballot drop boxes, limited weekend voting, and criminalized distributing food and water to voters in line. Florida's SB 90 restricted vote-by-mail access and imposed new ID requirements for ballot requests. The pattern is consistent: states use federalism's flexibility to restrict the franchise of populations that threaten incumbent power. Without a strong federal rights floor, federalism becomes the mechanism through which democratic backsliding occurs — not despite local control, but through it.

**State preemption of local democracy.** A growing pattern of state legislatures preempting municipal authority has created what the National League of Cities calls "the most significant threat to local self-governance in a generation." The pattern is systematic: cities enact progressive policies (minimum wage increases, paid leave, tenant protections, plastic bag bans, gun safety measures, LGBTQ+ nondiscrimination ordinances), and state legislatures, often gerrymandered to over-represent rural constituencies, pass preemption laws that void those local decisions. The Grassroots Change Preemption Watch project documents over 500 instances of state preemption of local authority since 2000. Twenty-five states have preempted local minimum wage laws; 43 have preempted some form of local gun regulation; 21 have preempted local nondiscrimination ordinances; 18 have preempted local rent control. The irony is structural: the same state legislators who invoke federalism and "local control" when resisting federal standards actively destroy local control within their own states. Research by the Economic Policy Institute shows that state preemption of local minimum wages has cost workers in preempted cities an estimated $1.5 billion annually.

**Fragmented governance as service failure.** The United States has approximately 90,000 units of local government — more than any other developed country by a large margin. This fragmentation produces duplicated services, uneven quality, fiscal competition between jurisdictions (the "race to the bottom" on taxes and regulation), and governance structures so complex that democratic accountability becomes effectively impossible. The average American is governed by 10-14 overlapping jurisdictions (city/town, county, school district, water district, fire district, transit authority, etc.), most of which hold separate elections with turnout rates below 15%. A citizen who wanted to attend every public meeting of every body that governs them would need to attend meetings multiple times per week — a practical impossibility. The result is governance without meaningful democratic participation: decisions about schools, water, policing, and infrastructure are made by bodies that most citizens cannot name, in meetings most citizens do not know occur.

**The interstate coordination gap.** Many of the most pressing policy challenges — climate change, water management, transportation, public health, labor standards — cross state lines and cannot be effectively addressed by individual states. The current federal system offers limited mechanisms for interstate coordination: interstate compacts require congressional consent (often delayed for years), regional planning agencies lack enforcement authority, and voluntary cooperation agreements have no binding force. The result is a governance gap where problems that are too large for states but too politically contentious for Congress fall through the cracks — addressed inadequately or not at all.

**The democratic consequence.** Federalism is not intrinsically democratic — it is a structural choice that can either enable or suppress democratic governance depending on its design. American federalism currently operates with too few rights floors (allowing states to suppress voting, civil liberties, and local authority), too much fragmentation (preventing effective governance and meaningful civic participation), and too little coordination capacity (leaving regional and national challenges unaddressed). Reforming federalism is not about centralizing power — it is about ensuring that every level of government operates democratically and that citizens have meaningful input at every level that affects their lives.

### Proposed Reforms

**9a. Subsidiarity principle with a non-derogable rights floor.**

Codify the principle that decisions should be made at the lowest effective level of government, with a constitutional rights floor that no state or local government can fall below. States retain autonomy on education, healthcare delivery, infrastructure, and economic development. They cannot opt out of equal protection, due process, voting rights, civil liberties, or minimum labor and environmental standards. The rights floor should be enforceable through private right of action in federal court — citizens should not depend on DOJ enforcement alone, which is vulnerable to political capture.

*Precedent*: Switzerland — 26 cantons, 2,131 communes, direct democracy at multiple levels. Citizens vote four times per year on average. Cantons have substantial autonomy on education, healthcare, policing, and taxation. But the federal constitution establishes non-derogable rights (Articles 7-36) that no canton can fall below, enforced by the Federal Supreme Court. The system has operated since 1848 with the highest civic trust in Europe and consistently ranks #1-3 globally for democratic quality (V-Dem, EIU Democracy Index). Germany's Basic Law (Article 28) requires that all state (Länder) constitutions conform to "principles of a republican, democratic, and social state governed by the rule of law" — creating a federal guarantee of democratic governance at every level. The EU's Charter of Fundamental Rights establishes a rights floor that member states cannot derogate from, enforced by the Court of Justice of the European Union — the most ambitious multi-level rights floor in operation. Canada's Charter of Rights and Freedoms (1982) overrides provincial legislation that violates fundamental rights, with the "notwithstanding clause" (Section 33) creating a narrow, politically costly mechanism for override. (See `from-scratch-design.md`, Model G4.)

*Evidence*: The contrast between states with strong rights floors and those without is measurable. States that maintained voting rights protections after Shelby County have higher turnout, more competitive elections, and greater policy responsiveness to median voter preferences (Hajnal, Lajevardi, and Nielson, 2017). States that have enacted restrictive voting laws since 2013 show measurable declines in turnout among affected populations — up to 7% in communities most affected by polling place closures (Texas LULAC analysis). The pattern holds across policy domains: states without minimum labor standards have lower wages, worse working conditions, and higher poverty rates even after controlling for economic structure.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing minimum standards in voting access, labor, environment, and civil liberties — modeled on the Voting Rights Act's enforcement mechanism but extended to other domains. Constitutional amendment for permanent protection. Estimated timeline: statute within 2-4 years; amendment over 10-20 years.

*Fiscal impact*: Negligible direct federal cost — the reform primarily constrains state action. Federal enforcement infrastructure (expanded DOJ Civil Rights Division, plus the racial equity office proposed in Domain 22d): $300-500M/year, funded through existing enforcement budgets. The fiscal return is in avoided costs: states that suppress voting rights and civil liberties impose costs on the federal system through increased litigation ($500M+ annually in voting rights litigation alone), reduced economic participation, and diminished tax revenue from excluded populations.

**9b. State-level laboratories of democracy.**

Encourage states to serve as testing grounds for structural reforms: proportional representation, citizens' assemblies, digital government, participatory budgeting, LVT pilots, ranked-choice voting. Establish a Federal Democratic Innovation Fund that provides matching grants (2:1 federal-to-state) for states that adopt evidence-based democratic innovations, with independent evaluation and public reporting of results. Success at state level creates demonstration effects that build federal momentum.

*Evidence*: Maine adopted ranked-choice voting (2018). Alaska adopted open primaries with RCV (2022). Oregon pioneered automatic voter registration (2015 — now adopted by 23 states). California and Michigan implemented independent redistricting commissions (2008, 2018). New York City implemented participatory budgeting (2011). Every major democratic reform in U.S. history started at the state or local level before going national — women's suffrage (Wyoming 1869 → 19th Amendment 1920), direct election of senators (Oregon 1904 → 17th Amendment 1913), marijuana legalization (Colorado/Washington 2012 → 24 states by 2024), marriage equality (Massachusetts 2004 → Obergefell 2015). The pattern is consistent: local success creates demonstration effects, builds public familiarity, and generates the political constituency for national adoption.

*Precedent*: Germany's federalism explicitly encourages policy experimentation (Experimentierklausel — "experimentation clauses" in federal law that allow states to deviate from federal standards to test alternative approaches, with mandatory evaluation). Finland's experimental legislation framework allows time-limited policy experiments with built-in evaluation — the universal basic income trial (2017-2018, 2,000 participants) was conducted under this authority. Canada's asymmetric federalism allows different provinces to assume different federal functions (Quebec administers its own pension plan, immigration selection, and income tax collection), creating natural experiments in governance approaches.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing the Democratic Innovation Fund. Modeled on the Race to the Top education fund (2009-2015), which successfully incentivized state-level education reform through competitive grants — but with democratic innovation rather than education as the focus. Estimated annual cost: $500M-1B in matching grants.

*Fiscal impact*: Democratic Innovation Fund: $500M-1B/year. The return is structural rather than fiscal: each successful state-level innovation builds the evidence base and political constituency for national adoption. Oregon's automatic voter registration, for example, has added over 500,000 new registrants and is estimated to have increased turnout by 4-6% — with effectively zero ongoing cost after implementation. The cumulative fiscal impact of democratic innovations (reduced corruption from participatory budgeting, more responsive fiscal policy from better representation, efficiency gains from digital government) is estimated at multiples of the investment, though precise estimates depend on adoption rates.

**9c. Municipal reform pathways.**

Many of the most impactful reforms — participatory budgeting, citizens' assemblies, open government data, digital services, ranked-choice voting — can be implemented at the municipal level without state or federal action. A coordinated movement of municipal reform creates facts on the ground that change what voters consider possible. Establish a National Municipal Innovation Network (modeled on the Bloomberg Philanthropies' What Works Cities program) to provide technical assistance, evaluation frameworks, and peer learning for cities implementing democratic innovations.

*Evidence*: The progressive era's most durable reforms — direct election of senators, women's suffrage, the initiative and referendum — all began as state and local experiments before going national. More recently: marijuana legalization started in two states (2012) and has spread to 24. Marriage equality was legal in 36 states before the Supreme Court nationalized it. Ranked-choice voting was used in one state (Maine, 2018) and has expanded to Alaska, New York City, and over 50 jurisdictions. The pattern is consistent — local success creates demonstration effects, builds public familiarity, and generates the political constituency for national adoption.

*Precedent*: Barcelona's "municipalism" movement (2015-present) demonstrated that a city government can implement radical democratic innovations — the Decidim digital deliberation platform (400+ organizations, 150,000+ participants), neighborhood assemblies, participatory budgeting, and civic technology infrastructure — that become models for national reform. South Korea's local government innovations, including the Suwon Citizen Governance initiative and the Seoul Innovation Park, have informed national policy. In the U.S., the Bloomberg What Works Cities program (2015-present) certified 97 cities for evidence-based governance practices, creating a peer network that accelerates adoption of proven approaches.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal support through the Municipal Innovation Network ($200M/year) plus technical assistance. Protection from state preemption (see 9d) is the essential precondition — without it, innovative cities face legal risk from hostile state legislatures.

*Fiscal impact*: National Municipal Innovation Network: $200M/year. The return: cities that adopt evidence-based governance practices consistently show improved service delivery, reduced costs, and higher civic engagement. New York City's participatory budgeting program allocated $300M+ through direct citizen votes, with independent evaluation showing high participation in low-income communities and spending decisions that better reflect community priorities than traditional budgeting. The overall fiscal impact of municipal innovation is multiplicative — each successful model reduces the political and technical cost of adoption for the next city.

**9d. Federal preemption reform.**

Establish clear principles for when the federal government may preempt state law, and — equally important — when it may not. Currently, federal preemption operates inconsistently: Congress preempts state consumer protection laws that exceed federal standards (benefiting industry) while allowing states to fall below federal labor, environmental, and civil rights standards (also benefiting industry). Reform should follow a one-way ratchet principle: states may exceed federal protections but may not fall below them. Federal legislation should include express preemption clauses rather than relying on implied preemption, and courts should apply a presumption against preemption in cases involving traditional state police powers.

Prohibit state preemption of local authority on core governance issues. A growing pattern of state legislatures preempting city-level minimum wages, paid leave, plastic bag bans, tenant protections, and gun regulations has created a "preemption penalty" that prevents the municipal experimentation that 9b and 9c rely on. Twenty-five states have preempted local minimum wage laws; 43 have preempted some form of local gun regulation; 21 have preempted local nondiscrimination ordinances. This is the federalism problem in reverse — and it must be addressed at both the state and federal level.

*Evidence*: The Grassroots Change Preemption Watch project documents over 500 instances of state preemption of local authority since 2000 — with the pace accelerating: more preemption laws were enacted between 2016 and 2024 than in the entire preceding decade. Research by the Economic Policy Institute shows that state preemption of local minimum wages has cost workers in preempted cities an estimated $1.5 billion annually. The National League of Cities has identified preemption as the single largest threat to local democratic governance. A 2023 study by the Local Solutions Support Center documented that state preemption laws disproportionately target policies enacted by cities with large minority populations — replicating the historical pattern of state legislatures overriding local governance in communities of color (Birmingham, Alabama's 2015 minimum wage increase was immediately preempted by the state legislature, which passed a law banning local minimum wage ordinances; the city is 70% Black).

*Precedent*: The EU's subsidiarity principle (Article 5, Treaty on European Union) provides the most developed framework for multi-level governance: the EU may act only when objectives "cannot be sufficiently achieved by the Member States" and can be "better achieved at Union level." National parliaments have a formal mechanism (the "yellow card" and "orange card" procedures) to challenge EU actions that violate subsidiarity. Germany's Basic Law (Article 30) establishes a presumption that state authority is primary unless specifically assigned to the federal level, with the Federal Constitutional Court actively enforcing this principle — but simultaneously prohibiting states from derogating from fundamental rights protections. Brazil's 1988 constitution explicitly protects municipal autonomy (Articles 29-30), including the right of municipalities to enact legislation on matters of local interest — making state preemption of municipal authority unconstitutional.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing the one-way ratchet principle and protecting core municipal authority. State constitutional amendments or ballot initiatives to protect local home rule in states where preemption is most aggressive. Model legislation has been drafted by the National League of Cities and the Local Solutions Support Center.

*Fiscal impact*: Negligible direct federal cost — the reform is primarily structural. The economic impact of reversing preemption is substantial: restoring local minimum wage authority alone would benefit an estimated 3-5 million workers and increase annual wages by $1.5-3B (EPI estimate). Local paid leave policies, where preempted, would cover an estimated 4 million additional workers. The democratic cost of preemption — measured in reduced civic engagement, lower trust in government, and suppressed policy innovation — is difficult to quantify but significant.

**9e. Interstate compact frameworks for regional challenges.**

Facilitate interstate compacts — constitutionally authorized agreements between states — as a mechanism for addressing challenges that cross state lines but do not require federal legislation. Priority areas: regional water management (already partially addressed by existing compacts but inadequate for climate change), multistate carbon markets (the Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative model), cross-border transit authorities, interstate professional licensing reciprocity, and coordinated emergency management.

Create a federal Interstate Compact Commission to provide technical assistance, mediation, and model compact language. Streamline the congressional consent process (currently ad hoc and often delayed for years) with expedited review for compacts that do not expand state power at federal expense.

*Evidence*: The National Popular Vote Interstate Compact — an agreement among states to award electoral votes to the national popular vote winner — demonstrates how compacts can achieve structural reform without constitutional amendment. Currently at 209 of the 270 electoral votes needed. The Driver License Compact (all 50 states) and the Interstate Compact for Juveniles (all 50 states) show that compacts can achieve universal adoption when the coordination benefits are clear. The Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative (RGGI), a compact among 12 northeastern states, has reduced power sector CO2 emissions by over 50% since 2005 while generating $7.4 billion in allowance auction proceeds invested in clean energy, energy efficiency, and consumer benefits — demonstrating that interstate compacts can achieve policy outcomes comparable to federal legislation.

*Precedent*: Australia's Council of Australian Governments (COAG, now the National Cabinet) provides a formal framework for inter-state coordination, with binding agreements on healthcare, education, and economic reform. Canada's Council of the Federation facilitates inter-provincial coordination, with binding agreements on healthcare transfers, labor mobility, and trade. The EU's "enhanced cooperation" mechanism allows a subset of member states to pursue deeper integration in specific policy areas — a model for allowing willing U.S. states to cooperate more deeply without requiring national consensus. Switzerland's inter-cantonal concordats cover higher education, police, and healthcare, with over 800 active inter-cantonal agreements creating what scholars call "cooperative federalism" — states solving shared problems without federal intervention.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing the Interstate Compact Commission and streamlining congressional consent. Executive order encouraging federal agencies to facilitate interstate cooperation on shared challenges. Estimated timeline: Commission operational within 2 years; first new major compacts within 3-5 years.

*Fiscal impact*: Interstate Compact Commission: $50-100M/year. The return is in coordination gains that neither federal nor individual state action can achieve. RGGI's $7.4 billion in clean energy investment demonstrates the fiscal scale that compacts can achieve. Professional licensing reciprocity (estimated to affect 30% of U.S. workers who require some form of occupational license) could reduce labor market friction by $6-12B/year in reduced relocation costs, increased labor mobility, and reduced occupational licensing barriers. Regional water management compacts, updated for climate change projections, could prevent billions in conflict and infrastructure duplication.

## Domain 10: Education

### The Problem

The American public education system is failing its democratic function. Public schools were designed to produce informed, capable citizens — people who can evaluate evidence, participate in civic life, hold institutions accountable, and collaborate across difference. Instead, the system produces vast inequality, chronic underfunding in the communities that need it most, declining civic knowledge, and a workforce inadequately prepared for the economy that actually exists.

**Funding inequality.** The U.S. funds public education primarily through local property taxes, creating a direct link between community wealth and school quality. The richest 10% of school districts spend 2-3 times as much per pupil as the poorest 10%. This is not a feature of all systems — most OECD countries fund education nationally or regionally with explicit equalization formulas. The U.S. system systematically underfunds the schools that serve the children with the greatest needs.

**Civic education collapse.** Only 9 states require a full year of civics education. Average civics proficiency among 8th graders has declined steadily since 2014, with the 2022 NAEP assessment showing the largest single-assessment decline in the test's history. A 2023 Annenberg survey found that only 47% of American adults could name all three branches of government. Citizens who cannot identify basic governmental structures cannot meaningfully hold those structures accountable.

**Politicized curriculum.** Education has become a primary theater of the culture war. Book bans reached record levels in 2023-2024 (4,240 titles challenged, per ALA). Legislation restricting classroom discussion of race, gender, and history passed in 18+ states between 2021 and 2025.
