Public defender parity with prosecution spending: $4–6B/year additional investment — with documented returns including higher acquittal rates for the innocent, reduced wrongful conviction costs (an estimated $1–2M in litigation and settlement per wrongful conviction), and shorter sentences that reduce mass incarceration costs. Net fiscal case: even accounting for full investment in defender parity and transition costs, decarceration is one of the highest-ROI reforms available.

**14c. Abolish cash bail and reform pretrial detention.**

Replace cash bail with a system based on risk assessment and release conditions. The default for nonviolent offenses is release on personal recognizance with appropriate conditions (check-ins, electronic monitoring for flight risk). Preventive detention is available for genuinely dangerous individuals, determined by judicial hearing within 48 hours with counsel present — but detention is based on evidence of danger, not inability to pay. Eliminate commercial bail bonds (the U.S. and the Philippines are the only countries that allow for-profit bail bonding). Fund pretrial services agencies to provide supervision, reminder systems, and support services that ensure court appearance without incarceration.

*Precedent*: New Jersey (2017) virtually eliminated cash bail, replacing it with a risk-based system. Results after five years: pretrial jail population dropped 44%, crime rates continued declining, and court appearance rates held steady at approximately 89%. Washington, D.C., has operated without cash bail since the 1990s — 88% of defendants are released pretrial, 88% make all court appearances, and 86% are not rearrested while awaiting trial. Illinois abolished cash bail in 2023 (the Pretrial Fairness Act). Kentucky, which implemented statewide pretrial risk assessment in 2011, saw its pretrial release rate increase from 70% to 85% with no change in public safety outcomes. Internationally, cash bail does not exist in most democracies — the UK, Canada, Australia, Germany, and France all use release-on-conditions as the default.

*Implementation pathway*: State legislation (New Jersey, Illinois, and New York have enacted comprehensive bail reform). Federal statute eliminating cash bail for federal offenses. Federal grant incentives for states that adopt pretrial reform (condition Byrne JAG funding on bail reform benchmarks).

*Fiscal impact*: Cash bail abolition is fiscally neutral to positive: the $14B/year commercial bail industry's revenue is replaced by savings in pretrial detention costs (estimated $14B/year nationally for detained-but-presumed-innocent individuals). Pretrial services agencies providing supervision, reminder systems, and support: $1–2B/year nationally for the full pretrial population. New Jersey's 5-year implementation results: approximately $300M/year in detention savings for a single state of 9 million people — projecting to $5–8B/year in net national savings while maintaining equal court appearance rates (89%) and equal public safety outcomes.

**14d. End private prisons and for-profit detention.**

Prohibit federal contracts with private prison and detention operators. Phase out existing contracts over 3 years as contracts expire. Prohibit states from contracting with private prison operators as a condition of federal criminal justice funding. Ban private operation of immigration detention facilities. The profit motive in incarceration creates a structural incentive to increase and extend imprisonment — private prison companies lobby for longer sentences, harsher immigration enforcement, and opposition to reform. CoreCivic and GEO Group, the two largest private prison operators, spent a combined $25 million on lobbying and campaign contributions between 2010 and 2024.

*Precedent*: President Biden signed Executive Order 14006 in January 2021, directing the DOJ to not renew private prison contracts for federal inmates. However, this did not cover immigration detention (the largest growth sector for private prisons) and was reversed by the subsequent administration. California (2019), Illinois (2019), Nevada (2019), and New York (2007) have banned or phased out private prisons at the state level. Internationally, the UK's Labour government announced in 2024 that it would not award new private prison contracts, and Israel's Supreme Court ruled private prisons unconstitutional in 2009 as a violation of human dignity. The evidence on private prison performance is clear: private facilities have higher rates of violence, worse conditions, higher recidivism, and no consistent cost savings (a 2016 DOJ Inspector General report found federal private prisons had 28% more assaults per capita than comparable public facilities).

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute prohibiting private prison contracts (the Private Prison Information Act and similar bills have been introduced in multiple sessions). Executive action (Biden EO 14006 demonstrated the mechanism). State legislation (California/Illinois model). Include immigration detention explicitly — currently the largest growth area for private incarceration.

*Fiscal impact*: Phase-out of federal and state private prison contracts requires no net new cost — public prison capacity must be maintained or built, but public facilities have lower operating costs per prisoner: the DOJ Inspector General's 2016 report found federal private prisons cost 8–14% more per prisoner than comparable public facilities when controlling for population risk. Eliminating private immigration detention (approximately 30,000–45,000 daily detained): $1.5–2B/year in current private detention contracts; replacement through public or non-profit facilities is estimated at equivalent or lower per-diem. The additional fiscal consideration: CoreCivic and GEO Group spent $25M in lobbying and campaign contributions 2010–2024 specifically to maintain and expand incarceration — an ongoing political cost that distorts policy toward greater detention regardless of public safety outcomes.

**14e. Restore voting rights and support reentry.**

Automatically restore federal voting rights upon release from incarceration (not upon completion of parole or probation — release from prison). Prohibit states from conditioning voting rights on payment of fines, fees, or restitution (which functions as a modern poll tax, as the Eleventh Circuit recognized in Jones v. Governor of Florida). Fund comprehensive reentry programs: transitional housing, job training and placement, substance abuse treatment, mental health services, identification document assistance, and record expungement for eligible offenses. Ban the box on federal employment applications (delay criminal history inquiry until after a conditional offer). Incentivize states to adopt automatic expungement for completed sentences on nonviolent offenses.

*Precedent*: Maine and Vermont never strip voting rights — incarcerated people vote from prison. Washington, D.C., restored voting rights for incarcerated residents in 2020. Twenty-three states and D.C. have restored voting rights for people on parole or probation (as of 2024). Minnesota (2023) restored voting rights to all people not currently incarcerated — approximately 55,000 Minnesotans regained the franchise. Internationally, the European Court of Human Rights ruled in Hirst v. United Kingdom (2005) that blanket felony disenfranchisement violates the European Convention on Human Rights. Canada's Supreme Court struck down prisoner disenfranchisement in Sauvé v. Canada (2002), holding that "the right to vote is fundamental to our democracy and the rule of law and cannot be lightly set aside." South Africa's Constitutional Court reached the same conclusion in Minister of Home Affairs v. NICRO (2004). The U.S. is an extreme outlier among democracies in the scope and duration of felony disenfranchisement.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute for federal elections (Congress has the authority under Section 2 of the Fourteenth Amendment to protect against vote denial). For state elections, advocacy for state-by-state restoration (Florida's Amendment 4, passed by referendum with 65% support in 2018, demonstrated bipartisan popular support — though the legislature's subsequent imposition of a fines-and-fees requirement demonstrated the fragility of ballot initiative reform without legislative goodwill). Federal funding conditions: tie criminal justice grants to automatic voting rights restoration upon release. Ban-the-box by executive action for federal employment (Obama EO 2015, partially reversed, should be made permanent by statute).

*Fiscal impact*: Federal voting rights restoration: near-zero direct cost — administrative modification to federal election law. DOJ Voting Section support for state-level restoration advocacy and litigation: $200–500M/year. Comprehensive reentry programs — transitional housing, job training, ID documents, substance use treatment, record expungement: $5–8B/year nationally. Return: Vera Institute data shows reentry investment returns $4–10 for every dollar through reduced recidivism costs ($35,000–60,000/year per avoided reincarceration), increased tax revenue from employed formerly incarcerated people, and reduced reliance on public assistance. Automatic expungement (Michigan, Pennsylvania model) costs $50–100 per case to administer and generates an estimated $15,000/year in additional earnings per expungee (Bushway/Weisberg research). The civic reintegration effect is also documented: people who vote are 30–50% less likely to reoffend (American Civil Liberties Union, 2017), suggesting voting rights restoration itself is a reentry tool.

**14f. Police use of force standards and de-escalation training.**

The five preceding subsections address accountability, sentencing, bail, private prisons, and civic reintegration — but leave a gap at the operational core: what standards govern police use of force, and what training equips officers to de-escalate rather than escalate? The United States has no federal use of force standard. Most agencies use a "reasonable officer" standard derived from Graham v. Connor (1989), which has been interpreted to give officers broad latitude. The result: U.S. police kill approximately 1,100–1,200 people per year, compared to Germany (8–12 annually, pop. 84M), the UK (fewer than 5, pop. 68M), and Australia (approximately 20, pop. 26M). Militarized tactics, inadequate de-escalation training, and the absence of national standards contribute to a use-of-force rate with no parallel in the democratic world.

The Camden, New Jersey case is the most instructive U.S. example. In 2012, Camden dissolved its police department entirely and rebuilt from scratch with a new department under county authority, new contracts without the qualified immunity shields the old contracts contained, and new training requirements emphasizing de-escalation, mental health response, and community policing. Results in the five years following: use of force incidents dropped 95%. Civilian complaints dropped 77%. Crime rates decreased. The city's 2020 police budget was redirected to community services. The Camden model is not universally applicable — it required dissolution, not reform — but it demonstrates that use of force rates are a policy variable, not an environmental constant.

**Proposed reforms for 14f**: Establish a federal use of force standard through statute: force is justified only when necessary to prevent imminent serious harm, with a proportionality requirement (the response must match the threat). Ban chokeholds, no-knock warrants, and the use of military weapons against peaceful protest by statute — not executive order, which can be reversed. Require that all law enforcement agencies receiving federal funding implement validated de-escalation training curricula (minimum 40 hours annually). Establish a Behavioral Health Response alternative to police dispatch: for mental health crises, substance abuse emergencies, and welfare checks, dispatch trained mental health responders instead of armed officers. These are the majority of calls in many jurisdictions — Denver's STAR program (mental health co-responder) handled 2,400+ calls in its first 6 months with zero police use of force incidents and zero arrests. Fund mental health co-response nationally as an alternative dispatch pathway.

*Precedent*: The UK's College of Policing sets nationally binding use of force standards and training requirements for all 43 constabularies — including mandatory de-escalation requirements, less-lethal alternatives hierarchy, and post-incident mandatory review. Germany's police training is a 3-year degree program with 100+ hours of de-escalation, communications, and social work components — compared to a U.S. average of 833 hours total training (less than half required to become a licensed cosmetologist in many states). Finland's police degree requires 3 years of study with emphasis on non-violent conflict resolution; Finnish police have killed fewer people total in the past decade than U.S. police kill in a single week. The Law Enforcement Action Partnership (LEAP), a group of current and former police, has documented that mandatory de-escalation training measurably reduces both use of force incidents and officer injuries.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal use of force standard (legislative framework): negligible direct cost. De-escalation training mandate (40 hours/officer/year, approximately 700,000 officers, at average training costs of $200/hour): $5.6B/year in training investment nationally. Behavioral Health Response infrastructure (community mental health co-response dispatch, modeled on CAHOOTS in Eugene, Oregon, which handles 24% of all calls at $2.1M/year for a city of 175,000): scaling to 330 million Americans = $4–8B/year nationally. Military equipment ban administrative enforcement: $100–200M/year. Total investment: $10–14B/year. Return: reduced civil liability costs ($3.2B/year currently in settlements), reduced long-term mental health costs from trauma for officers with excessive force incidents, reduced community-police conflict that depresses economic investment in affected neighborhoods. The RAND Corporation estimates that de-escalation training programs reduce use of force by 28–48%, reducing the human and financial costs of police violence by proportional amounts.

**14g. Prosecutorial ethics and accountability: closing the constitutional enforcement gap.**

The criminal justice system contains a structural accountability gap that subsections 14a through 14f do not directly address: the unchecked power of prosecutors. Police use force that is visible and can be filmed; judges make decisions on the record that can be appealed; legislators vote in public. Prosecutors make most of their consequential decisions — what to charge, what to offer in plea negotiations, what evidence to disclose, how to present testimony — in proceedings with no independent oversight and minimal public transparency. The result is a system in which the most powerful actor in the criminal justice process operates with the weakest external checks.

The evidence of the accountability gap is now extensive and documented at national scale. Official misconduct was involved in 71% of all fully overturned convictions in 2024, according to the National Registry of Exonerations (2024 Annual Report) — the largest single contributing factor to wrongful convictions, exceeding even eyewitness misidentification. Of the 147 exonerations that occurred in 2024, the registry added, 78% involved people of color and 35% involved "no-crime cases" — charges brought for conduct that was not actually criminal. The Innocence Project, which has secured 254 DNA-based exonerations since 1992, identifies Brady violations — the failure to disclose exculpatory evidence as required by Brady v. Maryland (1963) — as a contributing factor in over 50% of its cases.

The systemic nature of the problem was documented in the most comprehensive research conducted to date. A Notre Dame Law professor completed what is described as the largest-ever empirical study of prosecutorial misconduct in 2025, analyzing patterns across tens of thousands of cases. The cumulative findings establish that Brady violations are not isolated bad-actor incidents — they are a system-level failure pattern, reflecting how the adversarial process, docket pressure, and institutional culture of winning interact to produce systematic evidence suppression. A 2024 Vanderbilt Law Review analysis of Brady violations found that the most disturbing aspect is not the frequency of violations but how difficult it is for defendants to obtain justice after a violation is discovered: hard to win a new trial, hard to sue for money damages, hard to get policy change.

The enforcement gap at the disciplinary level compounds the problem. State bar associations nominally have authority to discipline prosecutors, but almost no disciplinary actions result from prosecutorial misconduct findings. In a 2003 study, the Center for Public Integrity identified 2,012 cases since 1970 in which a judge reversed a conviction, reduced a sentence, or dismissed charges due at least in part to prosecutorial misconduct — but only 44 of those cases resulted in any disciplinary review of the prosecutor. The APM Reports investigation found that of 15 state disciplinary agencies surveyed, only three tracked complaints against prosecutors separately from complaints against other attorneys — meaning most states have no mechanism to identify patterns of misconduct by specific prosecutors or offices. California's 2024 Annual Discipline Report, the most detailed in the country, shows that prosecutors account for a tiny fraction of disciplinary actions despite being the subject of a disproportionate share of judicial misconduct findings.

**Structural causes of the accountability gap**:

*Prosecutorial immunity*: Prosecutors have absolute immunity from civil suits for their decisions in initiating prosecution and presenting the government's case — a doctrine far stronger than the qualified immunity that applies to police. This immunity was established in Imbler v. Pachtman (1976) and has not been significantly narrowed. A prosecutor who knowingly presents false testimony, fabricates evidence, or deliberately withholds exculpatory material cannot be personally sued for damages, even after the wrongful conviction is established. The available remedy is the criminal conviction's reversal — which means the prosecutor suffers no personal consequences regardless of the severity of the misconduct. In contrast, under proposed reforms to qualified immunity (subsection 14a), police officers would face civil liability for constitutional violations. The legal asymmetry — greater accountability for officers than for the prosecutors who direct their work — is incoherent.

*Elected prosecutors and political incentives*: The United States is virtually alone among developed democracies in electing its prosecutors. Approximately 2,400 of the country's 2,477 chief prosecutors are elected, typically in partisan elections. The incentive structure is direct: elected prosecutors face career consequences for appearing "soft on crime" (which translates into acquittals or dropped charges) and essentially no consequences for wrongful convictions, excessive charging, or Brady violations. The culture of measuring prosecutorial success by conviction rate — rather than by justice rate — is a direct product of this electoral incentive structure.

*Docket pressure and resource asymmetry*: In the federal system and most state systems, prosecution is significantly better resourced than defense. The average public defender caseload exceeds 400 felonies per attorney per year (far beyond the ABA's 150-case recommendation), while federal prosecutors average approximately 50-75 active cases. This resource asymmetry creates the conditions for Brady violations: prosecutors managing large caseloads under pressure to win have neither the time nor the systematic incentive to search for and disclose exculpatory material that may cost them a conviction.

**The reform framework**:

*Open file discovery*: The single most effective near-term reform available. Open file discovery requires prosecutors to provide defense counsel with access to their complete case files — not just the evidence they intend to use, but all evidence collected. The Michael Morton Act in Texas (2013) — named after an Innocence Project client who spent 25 years wrongfully imprisoned for his wife's murder — requires open file discovery statewide. Research on open file states shows that Brady violations drop dramatically when defense attorneys can review the full prosecutorial file rather than relying on prosecutors' self-reporting of what is exculpatory. North Carolina, which adopted open file discovery in 2004, had the highest exoneration rate in the country shortly before — and has had measurably fewer post-conviction exonerations since. The Innocence Project's 2025 policy victories include successfully defending Texas's Michael Morton Act and New York's discovery reform law against industry attempts to roll them back.

The federal system does not have open file discovery. Federal prosecutors are required to disclose only specific categories of material under Rule 16 of the Federal Rules of Criminal Procedure and under Brady — a standard that relies entirely on the prosecutor's own judgment about what is exculpatory. A federal Open Evidence Act would require the DOJ to adopt open file procedures within 2 years, making the full investigative file available to defense counsel at least 30 days before trial.

*Brady tracking and enforcement*: Establish a federal Brady Violation Registry, maintained by the DOJ and cross-referenced with state bar databases. When a court finds that a Brady violation occurred, the finding is automatically reported to the registry. The prosecutor's office must respond within 60 days with a remediation plan. The registry is publicly searchable. Prosecutors with multiple findings receive enhanced supervision and mandatory training; offices with systemic patterns trigger DOJ Civil Rights Division investigation under 42 U.S.C. § 14141 (now 34 U.S.C. § 12601), which authorizes DOJ to sue for injunctive relief against law enforcement agencies engaging in patterns or practices of unconstitutional conduct. The consent decree framework (already used for police departments in Seattle, New Orleans, Chicago, and others) should be extended explicitly to prosecutorial offices.

*Limited prosecutorial liability*: Congress should carve back the absolute immunity doctrine for the most egregious cases: fabrication of evidence, knowing presentation of false testimony, and deliberate suppression of evidence the prosecutor knew was exculpatory. These are not judgment calls in the heat of litigation — they are intentional constitutional violations. Under a modified immunity framework (statutory, not requiring a constitutional amendment), prosecutors who commit these acts should be subject to the same civil liability standard that police face under the reformed qualified immunity regime in subsection 14a. The plaintiff still bears the burden of proving the violation; the reform eliminates the current rule that proving it is legally irrelevant to any personal consequence.

*State bar reform — mandatory tracking and reporting*: Federal conditions on prosecutorial grant funding (Byrne JAG, COPS) should require recipient states to: (a) establish a prosecutorial misconduct tracking system within their state bar disciplinary body, (b) receive automatic referrals from courts that find Brady violations or other prosecutorial misconduct, (c) publish annual statistics on complaints, investigations, and disciplinary actions against prosecutors, and (d) provide no statute of limitations below 10 years for misconduct that resulted in wrongful conviction. The New York State Bar Association's Special Committee on Prosecutorial Conduct has developed model tracking and disclosure procedures that can serve as the national template.

*Electoral incentive reform — toward merit selection*: As a longer-term structural reform, Congress should condition federal prosecutorial funding on adoption of non-partisan merit selection for chief prosecutors in jurisdictions with populations over 500,000 — transitioning away from partisan elected prosecution toward appointed, merit-selected district attorneys serving fixed, non-renewable 7-year terms with a removal mechanism for misconduct through an independent judicial commission. This is the model used in the UK (Crown Prosecution Service, with a Director of Public Prosecutions appointed by the Attorney General and confirmed by Parliament's Justice Select Committee), Canada (provincial Crown attorneys as career civil servants with professional appointment), Germany (Staatsanwälte as civil servants within the Justice Ministry with independent professional obligations), and France (parquetiers as members of the judiciary with constitutional independence protections).

*Innocence infrastructure*: Fund state Innocence Commissions — independent bodies with subpoena power, authority to review closed cases, and recommendations for post-conviction relief — in all states. North Carolina's Actual Innocence Commission (established 2002) was the first statewide body of its kind and has directly contributed to the identification and correction of wrongful convictions. As of 2025, only 18 states have any form of conviction integrity unit or innocence commission, and most lack adequate staffing and funding. A federal matching grant program ($1B/year) would fund conviction integrity infrastructure in all 50 states, with priority for states with the highest exoneration rates.

*Precedent*: The UK's Criminal Cases Review Commission (CCRC), established in 1997, is the gold standard for post-conviction innocence review. It has statutory authority to refer cases to the Court of Appeal, access to all police and prosecution files, and an independent staff of case reviewers. Since 1997, the CCRC has reviewed over 28,000 cases and referred over 700 to the Court of Appeal, resulting in approximately 450 quashed convictions. The Scottish Criminal Cases Review Commission (SCCRC) operates similarly. Australia's Law Reform Commission has recommended a national innocence inquiry body modeled on both commissions. New Zealand's Criminal Cases Review Commission became operational in 2020, with independent authority to investigate possible miscarriages of justice regardless of whether all domestic appeal rights have been exhausted.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal Open Evidence Act compliance (DOJ system development and training): $200-400M one-time, $50-100M/year ongoing. Brady Violation Registry (database, enforcement coordination, DOJ capacity): $100-200M/year. State bar tracking and reporting requirements (conditional funding): $50-100M/year in state administrative costs, offset by federal prosecutorial grant conditions. State Innocence Commission matching grants: $1B/year. Limited liability framework (statute, no direct fiscal cost — changes litigation exposure for DOJ). Total investment: approximately $1.2-1.5B/year. Return: each wrongful conviction that is avoided saves an estimated $1-2 million in direct costs (incarceration during wrongful imprisonment, litigation upon exoneration, compensation payments — 30+ states provide compensation of $50,000-$100,000 per year wrongfully incarcerated). The National Registry of Exonerations documents that wrongful convictions involved an average of 8-11 years of imprisonment; with approximately 3,000 known exonerations since 1989 and an estimated 2-5% wrongful conviction rate among the 1.9 million incarcerated, the scale of the problem — and the potential return on prevention — is enormous.

---

## Domain 15: Environment and Climate

### The Problem

The climate crisis is both an existential threat and a democratic one. The United States is the largest cumulative emitter of greenhouse gases in history, responsible for approximately 25% of cumulative CO2 emissions since 1750. As of 2026, the country has retreated from the Paris Agreement for the second time, dismantled key regulatory agencies, and opened new federal lands to fossil fuel extraction — while extreme weather events cost $92.9 billion in 2023 alone and are accelerating.

The environmental crisis is also a justice crisis. Communities of color are 1.75 times more likely to live near hazardous waste facilities. Low-income communities bear disproportionate pollution burdens. Indigenous communities face ongoing land and water rights violations. Rural communities dependent on extractive industries face economic devastation as those industries decline without transition support.

The institutional infrastructure for environmental protection is under direct attack. NOAA, the agency responsible for weather forecasting and climate science, has been targeted for dismantling. EPA enforcement actions have dropped to their lowest level in four decades. The Inflation Reduction Act's $369 billion in clean energy investment is being systematically clawed back through executive action. Scientific advisory committees have been purged. Career staff with decades of expertise have been forced out.

The structural failure is threefold: (1) regulatory capture by the fossil fuel industry has prevented adequate emissions reduction for decades, (2) the externalization of environmental costs onto communities without political power creates a persistent injustice that compounds over generations, and (3) the destruction of scientific capacity within government removes the ability to even measure the problem accurately.

### Proposed Reforms

**15a. Restore and strengthen environmental regulatory capacity**

Codify EPA's authority to regulate greenhouse gas emissions by statute — removing dependence on executive interpretation of the Clean Air Act that can be reversed every four years. Establish emissions reduction targets in law: 50% reduction from 2005 levels by 2035, net-zero by 2050, aligned with 1.5°C pathway. Restore and protect scientific advisory committees with statutory independence — committee members removable only for cause, with Senate confirmation for lead positions.

Rebuild NOAA as an independent scientific agency with statutory protections against political interference — modeled on the Federal Reserve's structural independence. Climate data, weather forecasting, and ocean monitoring are critical public goods that cannot be subject to political suppression. Fund NOAA at $8-10 billion annually (from current ~$6.3 billion) to address the satellite gap and expand extreme weather early warning systems.

*Evidence*: The EU's European Environment Agency operates with statutory independence from member state governments. Australia's dismantling of its Climate Commission in 2013 (and subsequent citizen-funded replacement as the Climate Council) demonstrates both the vulnerability and the public demand for independent climate science. The UK's Climate Change Committee, established by the Climate Change Act 2008, has statutory advisory authority that has survived multiple changes of government.

*Fiscal impact*: EPA reconstruction to full operational and enforcement capacity: $4–6B/year above current post-2025 funding levels. NOAA expansion to $8–10B/year (from current ~$6.3B): $1.7–3.7B/year additional. Total regulatory capacity restoration: $6–10B/year. Return: EPA's own retrospective analysis of the Clean Air Act found $30 in economic benefits per dollar of compliance cost — the highest documented benefit-to-cost ratio of any domestic federal regulatory program. Greenhouse gas regulations aligned with a 50%-by-2035 pathway avoid $190B/year in climate-related damages by 2030 (EPA modeling of mortality, agricultural loss, infrastructure damage, and extreme weather). The 2025 cost of NOAA weather forecasting capability degradation: impossible to fully quantify, but disruption to the National Weather Service warning system during a major hurricane season alone would cost more than the entire NOAA budget in excess mortality and uninsured property losses.

**15b. Environmental justice as a binding legal framework**

Elevate environmental justice from executive order (Clinton's EO 12898, subsequently rescinded and restored multiple times) to statutory right. Establish a federal environmental justice standard: no community shall bear pollution burdens more than 20% above the national average, with binding enforcement and a private right of action. Require cumulative impact assessment for all new permitting — not just individual facility review, but assessment of total pollution burden on affected communities.

Create an Environmental Justice Mapping and Enforcement Office with independent authority to block permits, require remediation, and impose penalties. Fund community-based environmental monitoring to give affected communities real-time data on air and water quality. Mandate meaningful community participation in permitting decisions — not pro forma public comment periods, but structured deliberation with veto power over siting decisions in overburdened communities.

*Evidence*: California's SB 535 and AB 1550 directed 35% of cap-and-trade revenue to disadvantaged communities and required cumulative impact assessment for permitting — the CalEnviroScreen tool has become a national model. New Jersey's 2020 Environmental Justice Law requires cumulative impact analysis and denial of permits in overburdened communities. The Navajo Nation's experience with uranium mining contamination — 523 abandoned mines, elevated cancer rates, and $17.8 billion in estimated cleanup costs — illustrates the generational cost of failing to protect vulnerable communities.

*Fiscal impact*: Environmental Justice Enforcement Office (independent agency with permitting authority, community monitoring grants, legal staff): $500M–1B/year. Community-based environmental monitoring network: $300–500M/year. Cumulative impact assessment requirements: $200–400M/year in additional federal regulatory administration. Avoided health costs from pollution reduction in overburdened communities: EPA's EJScreen tool identifies census tracts where diesel particulate alone causes $1,800–7,000/person/year in mortality and morbidity costs; across 5,000+ overburdened tracts averaging 5,000 residents, addressable excess harm exceeds $45–175B/year. The Navajo Nation's $17.8B uranium cleanup illustrates the compounding cost of delayed remediation: cleanup costs are orders of magnitude higher than prevention costs, and the burden falls on federal taxpayers who benefited none from the original extraction.

**15c. Clean energy transition with worker protection**

Establish a federal clean energy standard requiring 80% clean electricity by 2035 and 100% by 2045, with interim milestones and enforcement. Accelerate permitting for renewable energy and transmission infrastructure — the current 4-7 year average permitting timeline for transmission lines is incompatible with climate timelines. Create a Federal Transmission Authority with eminent domain authority for interstate transmission, modeled on the Interstate Highway System's construction authority.

Fund a comprehensive just transition program for fossil fuel workers and communities: wage replacement at 100% for up to 3 years, retraining with guaranteed employment in clean energy or remediation, pension and healthcare bridge funding, and economic diversification grants for fossil-fuel-dependent communities. The program must be automatic — triggered by facility closure announcements, not requiring workers to apply and navigate bureaucracy during crisis.

Establish a national climate bank (Green Bank) capitalized at $100 billion to provide low-cost financing for clean energy deployment, energy efficiency, and climate adaptation — particularly in communities underserved by private capital. The Connecticut Green Bank model, which leveraged $8 of private investment for every $1 of public capital, demonstrates the multiplier effect.

*Evidence*: Germany's coal phase-out commission (Kohlekommission) negotiated a 2038 exit with €40 billion in structural adjustment funds for affected regions — demonstrating that just transition is politically feasible when workers are guaranteed concrete protections. Denmark's wind energy transition created 33,000 jobs while reducing electricity-sector emissions by 70%. The Appalachian Regional Commission's experience shows both the need for transition support and the inadequacy of current programs — decades after coal's decline, many Appalachian communities still lack economic alternatives.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal clean energy standard (regulatory framework + enforcement): $500M–1B/year. Federal Transmission Authority and permitting reform implementation: $2–4B/year. Green Bank capitalization: $10B/year for 10 years, leveraging 5–10x private investment = $50–100B/year total annual clean energy financing at scale (Connecticut Green Bank achieves $8 private per $1 public). Just transition for fossil fuel workers: $2–4B/year for 100% wage replacement during retraining for approximately 40,000 annual coal/oil/gas job losses — modeled on Germany's €40B for 40,000 workers over 18 years. Solar and wind LCOE has fallen 89% and 69% respectively since 2010; every year of transition delay forfeits additional cost reductions and cedes clean energy manufacturing leadership. The Goldman Sachs analysis of the IRA estimated it would leverage $3T in private investment over 10 years from $369B in public provisions — a 8:1 ratio that makes clean energy transition among the most capital-efficient federal investments available.

**15d. Public lands protection and ecological restoration**

Establish a permanent moratorium on new fossil fuel leasing on federal public lands and waters. Current federal fossil fuel production accounts for approximately 25% of U.S. emissions — continued expansion is incompatible with any credible climate pathway. Honor existing lease obligations but prohibit extensions and new issuance.

Create a national ecological restoration program, investing $20 billion annually in ecosystem restoration: reforestation (targeting 60 million acres of degraded forestland), wetland restoration (4 million acres), grassland restoration, and coastal resilience. This is both a climate strategy (natural carbon sequestration of 500-800 million metric tons CO2 annually at scale) and an employment program (estimated 200,000-350,000 direct jobs in restoration, monitoring, and management).

Codify tribal co-management of federal lands with significant cultural and ecological value. The Bears Ears Inter-Tribal Coalition model — where five sovereign nations collaborate on land management with federal agencies — should be expanded to all public lands with tribal cultural significance.

*Evidence*: Costa Rica reversed deforestation from 21% forest cover in 1987 to 53% in 2021 through payment for ecosystem services and a fossil fuel tax dedicated to conservation — demonstrating that large-scale restoration is achievable within a generation. New Zealand's legal personhood for the Whanganui River provides a model for centering indigenous relationships with ecosystems in environmental law. The Civilian Conservation Corps employed 3 million workers during the New Deal and planted 3 billion trees — demonstrating the scale achievable through public investment in restoration.

*Fiscal impact*: Moratorium on new federal fossil fuel leases: near-zero direct cost; opportunity cost of foregone new lease revenue approximately $3–5B/year (existing leases honored, revenue stream preserved). National ecological restoration program at $20B/year: return from ecosystem services — the USDA Natural Resources Conservation Service estimates $200–500/acre/year in natural capital value (flood control, water purification, carbon sequestration, pollination, biodiversity). At 60 million acres of restored forestland and wetland: $12–30B/year in ecosystem services. Carbon sequestration value: 500–800 million metric tons CO2/year at $50–100/ton social cost of carbon = $25–80B/year in avoided climate costs. Employment: 200,000–350,000 direct jobs at approximately $50,000/year average compensation. Tribal co-management agreements: $500M–1B/year — generating irreplaceable ecological knowledge and preventing destruction of ecosystems that cost orders of magnitude more to remediate than to protect.

**15e. Climate adaptation and resilience**

Establish a national climate adaptation strategy with binding standards for infrastructure, land use, and emergency preparedness. Require climate risk disclosure for all federally funded infrastructure — no federal dollars for construction in 100-year flood plains without explicit climate-adjusted risk assessment and adaptation measures.

Create a Federal Climate Resilience Fund, capitalized at $50 billion over 10 years, for managed retreat, infrastructure hardening, and emergency preparedness in the most climate-vulnerable communities. Managed retreat — the planned relocation of communities from areas that will become uninhabitable — is the hardest climate policy challenge. Current federal flood insurance subsidizes continued habitation in high-risk areas; reform NFIP to phase out subsidized coverage in repeatedly flooded areas while funding voluntary buyout programs that are equitable and community-led.

Mandate climate-adjusted building codes nationwide for all new construction, with federal assistance for states and localities to enforce them. Current building codes in most jurisdictions do not account for projected climate conditions over the building's expected lifespan. Require federal infrastructure projects to be designed for projected 2070 climate conditions, not historical baselines.

*Evidence*: The Netherlands' Delta Programme, investing €1.4 billion annually in flood protection and climate adaptation, is the global gold standard for national-scale climate resilience planning — it operates on a 100-year planning horizon with binding commitments. Isle de Jean Charles, Louisiana — the first federally funded climate migration, relocating a community of mostly Biloxi-Chitimacha-Choctaw residents — illustrates both the necessity and the complexity of managed retreat. The 2021 Texas grid failure (Winter Storm Uri, 246 deaths, $195 billion in damages) demonstrates the catastrophic cost of failing to climate-proof critical infrastructure.

*Fiscal impact*: National Climate Adaptation Strategy (planning, federal standards, staff): $500M–1B/year. Federal Climate Resilience Fund: $5B/year for 10 years ($50B total). NFIP reform and managed retreat buyout program: $3–6B/year — replacing the current $1.7B/year average in subsidized flood insurance claims paid for properties rebuilt repeatedly in flood zones. Climate-adjusted building code adoption assistance: $500M–1B/year. Total climate adaptation investment: $9–13B/year. Return: FEMA estimates $6 in avoided disaster costs per $1 invested in pre-disaster mitigation — the clearest fiscal case in the entire climate domain. The CBO estimates climate damages will reach $100–200B/year by 2050 under current policy; this investment avoids a substantial share. The Netherlands' Delta Programme spends €1.4B/year protecting a €900B economy from existential flood risk — a 640:1 assets-protected-to-investment ratio that illustrates the logic of treating climate adaptation as insurance, not charity.

## Domain 16: Immigration and Citizenship

### The Problem

The United States immigration system is broken by design — not by neglect. It combines an enforcement apparatus with virtually no accountability, a legal immigration system so backlogged that lawful entry takes years to decades, and an interior enforcement regime that has evolved from targeted removal into mass surveillance and indefinite detention. The result is 11+ million undocumented residents living in permanent legal limbo, a $3.4 billion annual detention system that enriches private prison corporations while producing documented human rights violations, and an enforcement agency — ICE — that has undergone systematic mission creep from its 2003 founding into what the project's comprehensive research (`ice-detention-comprehensive-report.md`) documents as the largest immigration detention system in the world.

The current crisis has accelerated every failure. ICE's daily detention population has surged past 45,000 — a historic high. The "warehouse expansion program" has reopened shuttered facilities and converted military bases into detention camps. DOGE and Palantir have constructed a surveillance infrastructure that links immigration databases to Social Security records, state DMV databases, and local law enforcement systems. Deportation flights to third-party countries (including to CECOT in El Salvador) have proceeded without individualized hearings. The administration has attempted to strip birthright citizenship by executive order, denaturalize naturalized citizens, and use the Alien Enemies Act of 1798 for mass removal.

The structural failures are: (1) immigration enforcement operates outside the procedural protections that apply to criminal proceedings — no right to appointed counsel, no jury trial, administrative judges who report to the Attorney General, (2) the private detention industry has a direct financial interest in maximizing detention and lobbies accordingly ($5.3 million annually from GEO Group and CoreCivic combined), (3) the legal immigration system is so dysfunctional that it creates the undocumented population it then punishes, and (4) immigration policy has been weaponized as a tool of racial and ethnic targeting, from Chinese Exclusion to Operation Wetback to the Muslim Ban to current mass raids in Latino communities.

### Proposed Reforms

**16a. End mass detention; transition to community-based alternatives.**

Replace the current detention-first model with a community-based supervision system. Research consistently shows that community-based alternatives — case management, check-ins, GPS monitoring where necessary — achieve 99% appearance rates at court hearings at a fraction of the cost ($4.50/day vs. $150-$300/day for detention). Detention should be limited to individuals who pose a demonstrated flight risk or public safety threat, determined by a hearing before an independent immigration judge within 48 hours.

Prohibit the use of private for-profit facilities for any immigration detention. The profit motive in detention creates a structural incentive to maximize the detained population and minimize the quality of conditions. Existing private contracts — primarily with GEO Group and CoreCivic — should be phased out over 3 years, with no renewals. All remaining detention must meet enforceable standards with independent monitoring and a private right of action for detained persons.

*Evidence*: The Alternatives to Detention (ATD) program's own data shows 99% court appearance rates. The Vera Institute's evaluation of community supervision models found comparable compliance at 1/30th the cost. Sweden, Germany, and Canada all operate immigration systems with minimal detention and higher compliance rates. The GAO and DHS OIG have documented systematic failures in detention oversight — deaths in custody averaging 8-12 per year, medical neglect, sexual assault, and solitary confinement used as punishment — that demonstrate the current model cannot be reformed from within.

**16b. Independent immigration courts.**

Remove immigration courts from the Department of Justice and establish them as an independent Article I court, modeled on the U.S. Tax Court or the Federal Circuit's Bankruptcy Appellate Panels. Immigration judges currently serve at the pleasure of the Attorney General — who is simultaneously the chief law enforcement officer prosecuting the cases these judges hear. This structural conflict of interest has been documented by the National Association of Immigration Judges, which has sought independence for decades.

Independent immigration courts would have: fixed-term appointments with removal only for cause, caseload standards (current judges carry 1,500+ cases each — 4x what the NAIJ recommends), adequate staffing and support, and transparent precedent systems. Guarantee the right to appointed counsel for all persons in removal proceedings — the single reform most likely to improve outcomes. Currently, detained individuals without counsel are removed in 90%+ of cases; those with counsel succeed approximately 50% of the time.

*Evidence*: The American Bar Association, the Federal Bar Association, and the NAIJ have all endorsed Article I immigration court reform. Canada's Immigration and Refugee Board operates as an independent quasi-judicial tribunal with significantly higher due process standards. The current backlog exceeds 3.5 million cases — a direct consequence of inadequate judicial resources and political interference in case adjudication.

**16c. Comprehensive immigration reform with realistic pathways.**

Establish a 10-year pathway to permanent legal status for the 11+ million undocumented residents who have been living and working in the United States, with citizenship eligibility at year 13. Requirements: continuous U.S. presence, background check, tax compliance (including back taxes), English and civics requirements. No one should be required to leave the country and "get in line" — there is no functional line to get in. The family-based visa backlog for some countries exceeds 20 years. The employment-based system caps entire countries at 7% regardless of demand, creating absurdities like a 150-year wait for Indian nationals in the EB-2 category.

Reform the legal immigration system: eliminate per-country caps, expand employment-based immigration to meet actual labor market needs, clear the family-based backlog within 10 years, create a functional seasonal worker program with labor protections and a pathway to permanent status, and modernize the asylum system to process claims within 6 months.

*Evidence*: The CBO scored comprehensive immigration reform (the 2013 Senate bill) as reducing the federal deficit by $197 billion over 10 years and $700 billion over 20 years. Legalization of the undocumented population would increase GDP by an estimated $1.2 trillion over 10 years (Center for American Progress). The economic evidence is unambiguous: immigration reform is fiscally positive. The 1986 Immigration Reform and Control Act (IRCA) legalized 2.7 million people and produced no adverse employment effects for native-born workers.

**16d. Dismantle the surveillance-enforcement complex.**

Prohibit the use of immigration databases for purposes other than immigration processing. The DOGE-era integration of NUMIDENT (Social Security), SAVE, ELIS, and state DMV databases into a unified surveillance system has created an infrastructure for mass targeting that persists regardless of which administration controls it. Require warrants for ICE enforcement actions (currently, ICE operates primarily on administrative warrants signed by ICE supervisors, not judicial warrants). Prohibit 287(g) agreements and other programs that deputize local law enforcement as immigration agents — these programs have been shown to reduce crime reporting in immigrant communities, making everyone less safe.

End the use of facial recognition, predictive analytics, and AI-driven targeting in immigration enforcement. The Palantir-built systems currently in use (Investigative Case Management, FALCON, ImmigrationOS) create a surveillance infrastructure with virtually no oversight and documented racial bias. The detailed analysis in `algorithmic-decision-making-immigration.md` documents: ICM aggregates data from ENFORCE/IDENT (260 million biometric identities), SEVIS, TECS, commercial data brokers, and state law enforcement databases into a single platform with "predictive leads" — algorithmically generated enforcement suggestions whose specifications are proprietary and have never been independently audited. NIST's Face Recognition Vendor Test consistently finds false positive rates 10-100x higher for Black and East Asian faces. ISAP electronic monitoring (370,000 enrollees, the largest such program in U.S. history) uses automated facial recognition check-ins with known demographic bias for compliance scoring that can trigger re-detention.

Require mandatory Algorithmic Impact Assessments for all immigration enforcement systems (modeled on the EU AI Act's high-risk classification and Canada's Directive on Automated Decision-Making). Require disclosure of algorithmic risk scores to detained individuals and their attorneys in all custody determinations and bond hearings — extending the Houston Federation of Teachers v. Houston ISD precedent (algorithmic due process requires the ability to understand and challenge the algorithm) to immigration. Establish an independent algorithmic audit authority with access to source code, training data, and subpoena power. Impose a moratorium on facial recognition in immigration enforcement until NIST testing demonstrates demographic error rate parity.

*Evidence*: Studies in multiple jurisdictions show that 287(g) and Secure Communities programs reduce crime reporting by Latino residents (Insecure Communities report, 2013). The National Academy of Sciences found no evidence that immigration enforcement reduces crime. The ACLU's investigation of Palantir's ICE contracts documented systematic privacy violations and lack of oversight. Sanctuary city policies — which limit local cooperation with federal immigration enforcement — are associated with lower crime rates and higher economic output. Gonzalez v. ICE (filed 2024) is the first federal class action directly challenging algorithmic due process in immigration detention. The EU AI Act (2024) classifies immigration AI as "high risk" requiring bias testing, transparency, and human oversight — demonstrating that regulation is feasible.

**16e. Protect birthright citizenship and naturalization rights.**

Codify the Fourteenth Amendment's birthright citizenship guarantee by statute, creating a private right of action against any federal or state official who denies citizenship based on parents' immigration status. The current executive order attempting to end birthright citizenship has been enjoined by multiple courts but will reach the Supreme Court — statutory reinforcement provides a legislative backstop.

Strengthen naturalization protections: prohibit denaturalization except for fraud in the original application (not subsequent conduct), guarantee access to naturalization services within 6 months of eligibility, eliminate the backlog in USCIS processing, and fund community-based naturalization assistance programs. The right to become a citizen is the right to full membership in the political community — it must be accessible, not an obstacle course.

*Evidence*: The United States and Canada are the only major democracies with unrestricted jus soli (birthright citizenship). Proposals to restrict it would create a permanent underclass of stateless persons born on U.S. soil — a result incompatible with every democratic principle in this proposal. The denaturalization campaigns of the 2020s echo historical precedents (Japanese-American internment, Operation Wetback) that are now universally recognized as injustices.

---

## Domain 17: Labor and Employment

### The Problem

The American labor market has undergone a forty-year structural shift that has concentrated economic gains among capital owners while real wages for the median worker have stagnated. Union membership has declined from 35% in the 1950s to 10% today (6% in the private sector). The minimum wage has not been raised since 2009 — the longest gap in its history — and has lost over 30% of its purchasing power since its 1968 peak. The rise of the gig economy has created a new category of workers who are classified as independent contractors, denying them minimum wage protections, overtime, unemployment insurance, workers' compensation, and the right to organize.

These are not inevitable market outcomes. They are the result of policy choices: the weakening of the National Labor Relations Act's enforcement, the proliferation of right-to-work laws, the growth of mandatory arbitration clauses and non-compete agreements, the deliberate misclassification of employees as contractors, and the systematic defunding of workplace safety enforcement (OSHA has fewer inspectors today than in 1980, despite the workforce nearly doubling).

The labor crisis is also a democratic crisis. The decline of organized labor removed the single largest institutional counterweight to corporate political power. Unions historically provided not just workplace representation but civic infrastructure — voter registration, political education, community organizing, and a countervailing voice in policy debates. Their decline correlates directly with the rise of economic inequality, the stagnation of working-class political participation, and the vulnerability to populist capture that this proposal addresses throughout.

### Proposed Reforms

**17a. Sectoral bargaining.**

Establish a sectoral bargaining system where wage and benefit standards are negotiated industry-wide rather than workplace-by-workplace. Under the current enterprise bargaining model, workers must organize each individual employer — a process that takes years, faces systematic employer opposition (documented in 41.5% of organizing campaigns according to the EPI), and produces a fragmented patchwork of contracts. Sectoral bargaining sets minimum standards for entire industries through negotiation between worker representatives and employer associations, overseen by a government-appointed tripartite board.

*Evidence*: Nearly every wealthy democracy except the United States uses some form of sectoral or multi-employer bargaining. Germany's sectoral system covers 44% of workers (down from 80% but still far above U.S. levels). France's extension mechanism makes negotiated agreements binding on all employers in a sector, covering 98% of workers. Australia's Fair Work Commission sets industry-specific minimum standards. Nordic countries achieve the world's lowest inequality and highest quality of life through sectoral bargaining with 65-90% coverage. The Clean Slate for Worker Power project (Harvard/Roosevelt) proposed a detailed U.S. sectoral bargaining framework in 2023.

**17b. Federal minimum wage indexed to productivity.**

Set the federal minimum wage at $17/hour immediately (reflecting inflation since the last increase), then index it to median wage growth so it never falls behind again. Establish a separate tipped minimum wage at the full minimum (eliminating the $2.13 sub-minimum that has remained unchanged since 1991). Index all thresholds to the Bureau of Labor Statistics' Employment Cost Index, with automatic annual adjustments requiring no congressional action.

*Evidence*: Decades of minimum wage research — including the landmark Card and Krueger studies and subsequent replications across hundreds of state and local minimum wage increases — consistently find that moderate minimum wage increases do not reduce employment. States that have raised their minimums above the federal level (Washington, California, Massachusetts) have not experienced the job losses predicted by opponents. The $7.25 federal minimum has lost 30% of its real value since 2009; a worker earning it full-time makes $15,080/year, well below the poverty line for a family of two.

**17c. Worker classification and gig economy protections.**

Codify the ABC test for worker classification at the federal level: a worker is an employee unless the employer proves (A) the worker is free from control and direction, (B) the work is outside the employer's usual business, and (C) the worker has an independent business in the same trade. This addresses the systematic misclassification that denies protections to an estimated 10-30% of the workforce.

For platform workers (rideshare, delivery, task-based apps), establish a portable benefits system: pro-rated access to health insurance, workers' compensation, unemployment insurance, and retirement contributions funded by platform fees proportional to hours worked. Workers should not have to choose between the flexibility of gig work and basic economic security.

*Evidence*: California's AB5 adopted the ABC test in 2019 (later partially rolled back by Proposition 22, which is itself under legal challenge). The European Union's Platform Workers Directive (2024) established a presumption of employment for platform workers across all member states. The ILO estimates that 12.5% of the global workforce is now in platform or gig arrangements — this is not a niche problem. Washington state's 2022 legislation created a portable benefits system for rideshare drivers that provides minimum earnings, paid sick time, and workers' compensation without requiring employment classification.

**17d. Strengthen the right to organize.**

Pass a comprehensive labor law reform bill modeled on the PRO Act: prohibit captive audience meetings, establish card check recognition (majority sign-up), impose meaningful penalties for unfair labor practices (current penalties are trivial — back pay minus interim earnings, often years after the violation), ban permanent striker replacements, override state right-to-work laws, and extend NLRA coverage to agricultural and domestic workers (who were excluded from the original 1935 Act in a racially motivated compromise).

Fund the NLRB at levels adequate to process elections within 30 days and adjudicate unfair labor practices within 6 months. Current median time from petition to election is 40 days; median time to resolve an unfair labor practice complaint is over a year — delays that systematically advantage employers.

*Evidence*: The Wagner Act of 1935 produced the fastest growth in union membership in U.S. history — from 11% to 35% within 15 years — and coincided with the greatest period of shared prosperity in American history. The Taft-Hartley Act of 1947 and subsequent legal erosion reversed these gains. Countries with strong labor law enforcement (Germany, Scandinavia, Canada) have lower inequality, higher median wages, stronger safety nets, and more resilient democracies. The correlation between union decline and democratic vulnerability is not coincidental — organized labor is a structural check on oligarchic power.

**17e. Workplace safety, paid leave, and economic security.**

Rebuild OSHA: double the number of inspectors to reach the ILO-recommended ratio of 1 inspector per 10,000 workers (currently 1:80,000+), increase penalties to levels that actually deter violations, and extend coverage to public-sector workers in all states (currently excluded in 24 states). Mandate that OSHA develop standards for heat exposure, workplace violence, and ergonomic hazards — areas where rulemaking has been delayed for decades.

Establish federal paid family and medical leave: 12 weeks at 80% wage replacement, funded through a 0.4% payroll contribution (employee and employer each). The United States is the only wealthy democracy without national paid family leave — and one of only six countries in the world without it. Guarantee 7 days of paid sick leave annually for all workers.

Ban non-compete agreements for workers earning below $100,000 (indexed to inflation) and require that any non-compete for higher earners be compensated at 50% of salary for the duration of the restriction. An estimated 18% of American workers — including fast-food employees, janitors, and warehouse workers — are currently bound by non-competes that suppress wages and restrict mobility. Ban mandatory arbitration for employment disputes, restoring workers' access to courts and class action remedies.

*Evidence*: California has banned non-competes entirely and has the nation's most dynamic labor market. The FTC estimated that banning non-competes would increase worker earnings by $300 billion annually. Every other wealthy democracy provides paid family leave; the U.S.'s absence costs an estimated $28.9 billion annually in lost productivity and increased healthcare costs (NWLC). OSHA's current inspection capacity means the average workplace can expect an OSHA visit once every 165 years — enforcement at this level is not enforcement.

---

## Domain 18: Social Safety Net

### The Problem

The United States spends less on social protection as a share of GDP than any other wealthy democracy — 18.7% compared to the OECD average of 21.1% and Nordic averages above 25%. What it does spend is fragmented across dozens of programs with different eligibility rules, means-testing requirements, application processes, and administrative burdens that function as a de facto rationing system. TANF reaches only 21% of families in poverty (down from 68% when it replaced AFDC in 1996). The federal minimum SNAP benefit is $23/month. SSI benefits for disabled adults ($943/month in 2024) fall below the poverty line in every state. Unemployment insurance covered only 29% of unemployed workers in 2023 — the lowest rate since systematic measurement began. The child poverty rate spiked 139% in a single year when the expanded Child Tax Credit expired in 2022, the largest single-year increase in child poverty ever recorded, demonstrating both that the policy worked and that the political system chose to let it lapse.

The administrative burden itself is a policy choice. Eligible families spend an average of 7 hours navigating applications, provide documentation they have already submitted to other agencies, face recertification cycles that create coverage gaps, and lose benefits over paperwork failures rather than eligibility changes. The "welfare cliff" — where earning an additional dollar of income can reduce benefits by more than a dollar — creates poverty traps that punish work. These design features are not accidents. They are the residue of a deliberate political strategy, originating in the 1980s and intensifying through the 1996 welfare reform, to make public assistance maximally difficult to access and maximally stigmatized to receive.

### Proposed Reforms

**18a. Universal child benefit and childcare guarantee.**

Replace the current Child Tax Credit — which phases in with income and excludes the poorest families — with a universal child allowance of $300/month per child ($3,600/year), paid monthly, with no phase-in and no income test at the bottom. The 2021 expanded CTC demonstrated proof of concept: it reduced child poverty by 46% in six months, cut food insufficiency by 26%, and cost $105 billion annually. The current CTC costs $120 billion but excludes the 19 million children in families too poor to receive the full credit.

Establish a federal childcare guarantee: no family pays more than 7% of income for childcare, with direct subsidies to providers to maintain quality. Currently, childcare costs exceed rent in most states, and 51% of Americans live in childcare deserts. The lack of affordable childcare is the single largest barrier to labor force participation for parents — particularly mothers — and compounds every other inequality in the proposal.

*Evidence*: Canada's Canada Child Benefit (2016) reduced child poverty by 30% within two years. Australia, the UK, Germany, France, and every Nordic country provide universal or near-universal child benefits. The 2021 U.S. expanded CTC produced the fastest reduction in child poverty in recorded history. Québec's $7/day childcare program increased maternal labor force participation by 8 percentage points within five years and generated more tax revenue than it cost.

**18b. Guaranteed minimum income floor.**

Establish a federal guaranteed minimum income replacing the patchwork of TANF, SSI, and General Assistance with a single cash transfer that guarantees no American falls below 125% of the federal poverty line ($19,088 for an individual, $39,000 for a family of four in 2024 dollars). The benefit phases out gradually — at a 30% rate — eliminating the welfare cliff and ensuring work always increases total income. Consolidate the application process through the digital government infrastructure in Domain 4: one application, one verification, automatic enrollment where possible using existing tax data.

This is not Universal Basic Income — it is a guaranteed floor, targeted at those below or near poverty, replacing fragmented programs with a single coherent system. Current spending on TANF ($16.5B federal block grant, unchanged since 1996 and worth 40% less in real terms), SSI ($60B), and state general assistance could be redirected to fund a significant portion of the cost.

*Evidence*: Stockton's SEED program ($500/month to 125 randomly selected residents, 2019-2021) found recipients were more likely to find full-time employment (from 28% to 40%), had reduced income volatility, and reported improved mental health. Finland's basic income experiment (2017-2018) found no reduction in employment and significant improvements in wellbeing. Canada's Mincome experiment (1974-1979) found minimal work disincentive and significant reductions in hospitalization, domestic violence, and high school dropout rates. TANF's block grant structure has allowed states to divert funds away from cash assistance — in some states, less than 10% of TANF funds reach families as cash.

**18c. Food security as a right.**

Increase SNAP maximum benefits by 30% (the Thrifty Food Plan revision initiated in 2021 increased benefits by 21% — the first substantive increase since 1975 — but the benefit level still assumes cooking time and kitchen access that many recipients lack). Eliminate the 3-month time limit for able-bodied adults without dependents, which removes food assistance from people who are working but cannot find sufficient hours, are between jobs, or face barriers to employment that do not meet the narrow disability definition. Establish universal free school meals — permanently extending the pandemic-era policy that 10 states have now adopted independently — and Summer EBT for all children in participating households.

Index SNAP benefits to the Low-Cost Food Plan rather than the Thrifty Food Plan, which USDA's own researchers have acknowledged does not reflect realistic diets. Expand SNAP eligibility to include college students (currently excluded unless working 20+ hours/week) and formerly incarcerated individuals (currently subject to lifetime bans in some states for drug felony convictions — a policy that increases recidivism by making re-entry harder).

*Evidence*: SNAP is the most effective anti-poverty program in the U.S. after Social Security, lifting 3.4 million people out of poverty in 2022. Every $1 in SNAP spending generates $1.50-$1.80 in economic activity. Universal free school meals in California and Maine reduced absenteeism and eliminated the stigma of means-tested lunch programs. The drug felony SNAP ban — affecting an estimated 300,000 people — has no evidence of deterring drug use and significant evidence of increasing food insecurity and recidivism.

**18d. Unemployment insurance modernization.**

Establish a federal floor for unemployment insurance: minimum 26 weeks of benefits at 60% of prior wages (up to a cap of $1,000/week). Currently, 12 states provide fewer than 26 weeks, and Florida's maximum benefit is $275/week for 12 weeks — designed to discourage claims rather than support workers. Create automatic extended benefits that trigger when state unemployment exceeds 6.5%, eliminating the political negotiation that delayed pandemic UI extensions and let benefits lapse during the 2008 recession. Extend coverage to gig workers, independent contractors, and part-time workers who currently lack UI eligibility despite paying into the system through alternative work arrangements that Domain 17's worker classification reforms will address.

Modernize state UI technology: 25+ states still run their UI systems on COBOL mainframes from the 1970s, which caused catastrophic processing failures during the pandemic. Fund technology modernization through the digital government infrastructure in Domain 4, with interoperable systems that can handle surge demand.

*Evidence*: During the pandemic, the $600/week federal supplement reduced poverty among unemployed workers by 47%. States with more generous UI benefits have faster economic recoveries from recessions because benefits are spent immediately in local economies. Germany's Kurzarbeit (short-time work) system, which subsidizes reduced hours rather than full layoffs, preserved 2.2 million jobs during the pandemic at lower cost than the U.S. approach — a model for automatic stabilizers that prevent unemployment rather than merely compensating for it.

**18e. Disability benefits reform.**

Increase SSI benefits to at least 100% of the federal poverty line (currently 75%) and index to inflation annually. Eliminate the $2,000 asset limit for SSI recipients — a limit that has not been adjusted since 1989 and forces disabled Americans to remain in poverty to keep their benefits. The asset limit prevents saving for emergencies, homeownership, or retirement, trapping disabled people in permanent economic precarity. Raise the limit to $10,000 immediately and eliminate it within five years.

Streamline the disability determination process: the average wait for an SSA disability hearing is 19 months, during which applicants — who by definition cannot work — have no income. Increase the number of administrative law judges and implement the digital infrastructure improvements from Domain 4 to reduce processing times to 90 days. End the five-month waiting period for SSDI benefits and the two-year waiting period for Medicare eligibility after SSDI approval — waiting periods that force disabled workers into poverty and delay healthcare access during the period they need it most.

Reform the disability determination criteria to reflect modern labor markets: the current system evaluates whether a claimant can perform "any work that exists in the national economy," a standard that ignores geography, transportation, and the actual availability of accommodating employers. Criteria should assess whether the claimant can realistically obtain and sustain employment given their functional limitations and local labor market.

*Evidence*: The SSI asset limit has not been updated in 35 years; adjusted for inflation, the 1989 limit would be approximately $5,000 today. The ABLE Act (2014) acknowledged this problem but caps contributions and creates administrative complexity. Countries with higher disability income floors (Netherlands, Sweden, Denmark) have better employment outcomes for disabled people — because adequate benefits provide stability for rehabilitation and job search, while poverty creates stress that worsens health and reduces functioning. The 19-month average hearing wait represents a system failure: 10,000 people die annually while waiting for their disability hearings.

---

## Domain 19: National Security and Foreign Policy

### The Problem

The United States spends $886 billion annually on national defense — more than the next ten countries combined, and more than 40% of global military spending. This spending is largely insulated from democratic accountability: defense budgets pass with overwhelming bipartisan support, the Pentagon has never passed an independent audit (failing its sixth consecutive audit in 2023), and the Authorization for Use of Military Force passed in 2001 has been used to justify military operations in at least 22 countries across four administrations without further congressional vote. The military-industrial complex that Eisenhower warned about in 1961 has matured into a system where defense contractors spend $124 million annually on lobbying, employ 700+ former government officials, and maintain facilities in strategically distributed congressional districts to ensure political protection for weapons programs the military itself has not requested.

Meanwhile, the State Department — responsible for the diplomacy that prevents wars — receives 6% of the defense budget. USAID, which funds development programs that address root causes of instability, faces elimination. Intelligence community oversight has eroded since 9/11, with surveillance programs repeatedly found to exceed their legal authorization. Veterans — 18 million Americans who served — face a VA system that, despite improvements, still has average wait times of 26 days for primary care and 42 days for mental health appointments, while veteran suicide remains at 17.5 per day.

The current crisis has compounded these problems. The administration has withdrawn from multilateral agreements, threatened NATO allies, praised authoritarian leaders, and used military resources for domestic enforcement operations (border militarization). Arms sales to authoritarian regimes continue with minimal congressional oversight. The national security apparatus — designed to protect citizens from external threats — is being redirected toward domestic political objectives, while actual security threats (climate change, cyber warfare, pandemic preparedness, great power competition) receive inadequate strategic attention.

### Proposed Reforms

**19a. War powers restoration and military authorization reform.**

Repeal the 2001 and 2002 AUMFs and replace them with a framework that requires specific, time-limited, geographically bounded authorizations for the use of military force. No authorization may exceed two years without re-vote. The president retains the constitutional authority to respond to imminent attacks, but must seek congressional authorization within 48 hours and cease operations within 60 days without it — restoring the War Powers Resolution's original intent, which every president since Nixon has treated as advisory.

Establish automatic sunset provisions: any military deployment not reauthorized by Congress after 24 months ends. Require that all authorization votes include a cost estimate from CBO and an assessment of civilian casualty risk from an independent body. Prohibit the use of military force authorization to justify surveillance, detention, or enforcement operations against U.S. citizens or residents.

*Evidence*: The 2001 AUMF — a 60-word authorization passed three days after 9/11 — has been cited to justify military operations in Afghanistan, Iraq, Syria, Yemen, Somalia, Libya, Niger, and at least 14 other countries. Congress has not voted to authorize most of these operations. The founders explicitly assigned war-declaration power to Congress (Article I, Section 8) because they understood that concentrating war authority in the executive invited perpetual conflict. Every other established democracy requires legislative authorization for military operations beyond immediate self-defense.

**19b. Defense spending accountability and reallocation.**

Require the Department of Defense to pass a clean financial audit within five years or face automatic 1% annual budget reductions until compliance is achieved. The Pentagon has received $8.5 trillion in appropriations since 2001; its six consecutive audit failures mean that no one — including the Pentagon itself — can account for how this money was spent.
